Claude Code Head Boris Cherny: Insane Growth, Tokenmaxxing, AI Agents' Next Frontier

Alex Kantrowitz(55.4K)·57:24·6.9K views·2026-05-20
Boris ChernyHead of Claude Code, Anthropic Alex KantrowitzHost, Big Technology
TL;DRAnthropic’s Claude Code and co-work agentic products are experiencing explosive, exponential growth, shifting AI from basic conversational chatbots to autonomous agents that can control computers and use APIs.
TL;DR Anthropic’s Claude Code and co-work agentic products are experiencing explosive, exponential growth, shifting AI from basic conversational chatbots to autonomous agents that can control computers and use APIs. This shift has enabled immense productivity gains, with...

TL;DR

Anthropic’s Claude Code and co-work agentic products are experiencing explosive, exponential growth, shifting AI from basic conversational chatbots to autonomous agents that can control computers and use APIs. This shift has enabled immense productivity gains, with Anthropic engineers writing 250% more code, even as the company scales its infrastructure to meet unprecedented demand.

Unprecedented Hypergrowth and Self-Generating Code

  • Exponential Growth Metrics: Anthropic is experiencing massive scaling, with CEO Dario Amodei previously reporting an 80x year-over-year increase in demand.
      • While Anthropic’s ARR was at $4 billion last year, host Alex Kantrowitz noted that current market rumors suggest ARR has skyrocketed toward $45 billion.
      • Cherny stated that since the release of Opus 4 and Sonnet 4 in May of last year, followed by updates 4.5, 4.6, and 4.7, product growth has inflected repeatedly at a pace unlike anything he has witnessed in his tech career.
  • Coding Productivity Gains: The volume of code written per engineer at Anthropic has increased by approximately 250% since the introduction of Claude Code, with code quality and system reliability remaining entirely stable.
  • Self-Building AI: In a remarkable recursive feedback loop, Claude Code and its sister product co-work are now 100% written by Claude Code.
  • Widespread Adoption: At a recent Y Combinator talk, Cherny asked a room of several hundred founders how many write 100% of their software using Claude Code; nearly half of the room raised their hands.

The Shift from Chatbots to Tool-Using Agents

  • The Agent Paradigm: Unlike early-generation chatbots, Claude Code and co-work operate as autonomous agents. They do not just predict text; they use computer tools, browse the web, edit local files, and hook into external APIs (such as QuickBooks for small business bookkeeping).
      • Cherny highlighted a personal use case where co-work navigated his email and calendar, caught scheduling mistakes, and autonomously booked eight flights and five hotels across a complex five-city itinerary.
  • The Rise of Parallel Workflows: Power users are no longer running just one Claude instance at a time. Cherny noted that he routinely runs five instances simultaneously during the day, and up to hundreds or thousands of Claudes in parallel overnight to execute complex tasks.
  • Auto Mode and Safety: To combat prompt fatigue—where users blindly click "approve" on safety dialogs—Anthropic introduced "Auto Mode."
      • Autonomous Safety Routing: Instead of asking the user for permission for every individual action, Claude asks a secondary Claude instance if a tool call is safe to make, utilizing thousands of background benchmarks. Tests show this secondary-agent validation is actually safer than manual human approval.

Addressing "Tokenmaxxing" and Infrastructure Constraints

  • The Tokenmaxxing Phenomenon: Reports have emerged of employees at major tech firms (like Amazon) purposefully running useless, hours-long automated AI loops to artificially boost their internal AI usage metrics to meet corporate mandates.
      • Cherny's Perspective: Cherny does not believe tokenmaxxing represents a significant share of overall demand. He advises companies to offer "psychological safety" and free token allotments to encourage organic workflow experimentation rather than gamifying usage through leaderboards.
  • The "90s PC" Productivity Parallel: Cherny compared the current AI transition to the 1990s adoption of personal computers. Companies back then bought PCs but saw no productivity gains until they threw out their physical filing cabinets and redesigned their entire business processes around the computer. AI requires the same structural reorganization.
  • Resolving Rate Limits: Heavy users often complain of hitting strict rate limits. To resolve this, Anthropic has doubled its 5-hour rate limits, increased weekly limits, and onboarded massive new server capacity via Elon Musk’s "Colossus" supercomputer cluster.

Redefining Business Moats and the "World Model" Debate

  • Declining vs. Growing Moats: Evaluating AI's impact on software through the "Seven Powers" framework, Cherny predicts a shift in corporate defensibility:
      • Switching Costs Will Decrease: AI agents make it incredibly easy to port data and switch from vendor A to vendor B, eroding traditional software lock-in.
      • Network Effects and Economies of Scale Will Strengthen: Structural moats like proprietary user networks and physical manufacturing scale (e.g., TSMC or massive cloud infrastructure) will become even more valuable.
  • The World Model Debate: Meta’s Yann LeCun has argued that LLMs cannot make reliable agents because they lack a "world model" and cannot predict the consequences of their actions.
      • Cherny's Counter-Argument: Cherny strongly disagrees with LeCun's skepticism, pointing to Claude's surprising ability to plan, reason, and look ahead (such as planning the second line of a poem while generating the first). Cherny issued an open invitation for LeCun to sit down and use Claude Code for an hour to see its planning capabilities firsthand.

Notable Quotes

"I don't write code. I prompt Claude. And actually nowadays, mostly what I'm doing is I have a Claude that prompts other Claudes. So I don't even talk to Claude. I have a Claude that's talking to my Claudes." — Boris Cherny

"I would put out an offer to Yann [LeCun], if he wants to sit down and Claude Code together for an hour, I'd love to show him. And then I'm curious to hear what he thinks." — Boris Cherny

Most things in the world we will not build. And so this is why we also have to provide a platform, managed agents, an API, and an SDK—all these products so people can build on top. Thousands and thousands of businesses choose to do that. Yeah, it's interesting to hear you even answer the question saying that it's a mix. Now, we've set the stage here that this is something that's growing exponentially. We obviously have seen the Anthropic revenue grow exponentially kind of alongside this product. This is a product that you conceived of, built, and run today. I think that there's probably some people watching who are like, "Well, what is Claude Code?" Most of our viewers obviously know what it is. I was thinking, how do I write this in a simple one-sentence definition? And I wrote that it's a way to build websites and software in plain English. I think when a lot of people think about AI, they think about chatbots. For engineers, that's what AI was, maybe like a year and a half ago before we started Claude Code. That's what AI was for most people. We realized at some point that the model was actually getting really good at coding and it's getting really good at using tools. These are things that we've always trained the model to do. This has been the research direction for a while. It started to become commercially useful about a year and a half ago. And so for Claude Code, we took this bet. We deviated from the way that everyone wrote code at the time. Because the way that everyone in the world wrote code was using essentially a fancy text editor, and we just thought maybe we can do much better than this and do something really, really different than what's been done before. It was very much a bet. So we introduced Claude Code, and the thing that made Claude Code different from chatbots at the time was Claude Code can use tools. And this is it—this is just the difference. With a chatbot you're going back and forth and talking, but an agent—and Claude Code is an agent—can use your tools. Right. So it becomes less of "what does this product do itself" and more of "what can this product log into and then sort of do with a multiplicity of products that you use online." Yeah, that's right. It can connect all your different tools. It can use your browser, it can use your computer, even something as simple as editing a file on your computer. A year and a half ago, there was no AI product that could actually do that. But this is the first thing that Claude Code was able to do: it could edit a file on your desktop. If you have a bunch of files on your desktop, it can organize them. So Claude Code and co-work have this access, if you choose to give it to them. Sure, if granted. Yeah, and you know, it can do this. This is magical. This tiny difference completely changes the way that people can use this product and it totally changes what this product can do for you. Yeah. I mean, the fundamental thing, just to drill down here, is that it seems like AI has shifted from sort of like, "AI is great at autocomplete," right? Because at the fundamental layer, AI is just predicting what comes next. It's predicting, if you're using machine learning and applying it on a large dataset, predicting whether you might default on your mortgage and whether a bank should grant a mortgage. When it comes to a sentence, predicting the next word. With code, predicting the next bit of code in a sequence, right? So I think that was Gen 1. But what you're talking about now is the machine is actually just able to go and, after you give it this natural language prompt, code itself, hook into tools, and then do things for you. And so, correct me if I'm wrong, but the use cases here have gone from developers hooking into it and writing code with Claude Code—and we've seen this explosion I guess largely driven by them—but then by a secondary force: by non-technical folks, people like me, who can build software by directing the AI agent, which is Claude Code, to build a piece of workflow software for them, or a website, or to take control of your computer via something like Claude co-work, which is sort of the, maybe I would call it, the easier sister product, and saying, "Well, you have access to my browser now. But now I want to pressure test the thesis a little bit and bring up some things that make me curious how much of this is real and how much of this is just unbridled enthusiasm at the potential, but maybe stuff we should have a reality check on. The first thing is that there's such great demand, but the question is how much of that demand is pure demand versus demand that's gamified. There is a practice going on within Silicon Valley and outside of it that's called tokenmaxxing. I'm sure you've heard of it. It's where companies have a mandate where people are supposed to use lots of AI tokens by running their AI agents as much as they can, and then those who use the most tokens are rewarded on a leaderboard or meet a goal of AI actions that they have to take as opposed to physical actions. So I want to hear your perspective on tokenmaxxing and whether you think that makes up a large portion of the usage of the products that you're building. Yeah, I don't think tokenmaxxing is a large percent. The way that I would think about it is, before Anthropic actually, I used to work at a big tech company. You were at Facebook. I was at Facebook. Which is one of the companies that's tokenmaxxing. That's right, that's right. And one of my responsibilities was the health of all of the code across Meta's apps. So this is like Facebook, Instagram, and WhatsApp. One of the reasons that we care about the health of the code, which is essentially things like code quality, is if the code is really high quality, engineers are more productive. There was a big team of people that worked on productivity. Before models, before Claude, you would work for a really long time and you would see maybe a 1, 2, or 3 percent improvement in productivity per engineer over the course of a year. Something like that. That was like a pretty big improvement, and it was very hard-won. You essentially had to try a lot of ideas and eventually you would find something that improved productivity like this. What happened with Claude is now many companies, including Anthropic and all of our biggest customers, are reporting gains on the order of hundreds of percentage points. I think the last number that we reported is that the amount of code written per engineer at Anthropic has grown something like 250% since we introduced Claude Code. This is while keeping code quality and reviewability and all these things stable. So without those things regressing, the volume of code has grown a lot. This kind of productivity impact, I think, is just very new. People are trying to figure out how we get this. There's a lot of companies asking, "How do we get these kinds of benefits?" because a lot of companies are seeing it and then some are still figuring it out. My advice is almost always the same. The first thing is just give everyone tokens; let people experiment. I wouldn't necessarily recommend tokenmaxxing, but I would recommend letting people experiment so they don't have to ask for approval for every token. The second thing is give people psychological safety. A lot of times when people are innovating and they're building tools that make them more productive, they're changing their own workflows to make themselves more productive. They try a bunch of ideas; some of them might not work, and then some of them work. You want to give people this kind of psychological safety so they feel okay experimenting with it and finding these new processes. And then the thing that we're realizing is actually productivity improvements and innovations do not come from the people you expect. Back in the old days, everyone could point out, "These are my most productive engineers." But nowadays, a lot of the improvements are coming from people you just never would expect. It could be like an accountant somewhere in the corner of your org who just automates accounting in a way that no engineer would have thought of. It could be some marketer automating marketing in a way that you never would have thought of. It could be a new-grad software engineer that just built something amazing. This is something that just didn't happen before. The challenge is you can't identify these engineers and these people ahead of time. You don't know who they are. It's almost always going to surprise you. And so the thing you want to do is let people experiment, give them safety, and then once there's some kind of use case that scales up, that's when you think about optimizing it. But you don't want to optimize ahead of time. So, I don't know, if doing it in a competitive way works for some companies with their culture, then I think that's great. If for other companies the way they want to do it is just to create safety and create space for engineers to experiment, which is what we do at Anthropic, then I think that's great too. It really depends on the company. Yeah. And I'll say look, I use a lot of tokens. I'm in the tools all the time. I think Claude Code and Claude co-work have both been pretty great for my business. I'm a solo operator—although that kind of sells it short because I have a team of people behind me that help me, mostly on a part-time basis, but that's for a different show. But I do wonder, when I read these stories, the large corporations are largely making up big, big percentages of these budgets, and the incentives—and again, like I started the show saying, how sustainable is this—the incentives are bad in some of these places. This is from the Financial Times recently: "Amazon staff use AI tool for unnecessary tasks to inflate usage scores. Some employees said colleagues were using the software to automate additional unnecessary AI activity to increase their consumption of tokens. They said the move reflected pressure to adopt the technology after Amazon introduced targets for more than 80% of developers to use AI each week." I've gut-checked this with an Amazon employee, and they're like, "Yep, this is what's happening." They told me, "I triggered an automation that runs for hours and then gets deleted every day in order to meet these targets." So you said you don't think that this tokenmaxxing stuff is a big part of demand. Is there anything that you can see on your end to indicate that it's not, that this is an outlier and not the rule in most places? Yeah, I don't know how many companies are doing this tokenmaxxing thing. I've heard of it as a trend, you know, a little bit. If you look at Claude Code's customers, we just have many, many, many customers. It's not like there is one company driving the usage; it's not like that. I do want to kind of step back a little bit and just think about how this kind of change happens. I think the goal of what these companies are trying to do—I don't want to speak for them, and I would recommend just talking to them—but the goal of what they're trying to do is probably organizational change and business process change. How do you make it so your company benefits from AI? This is often unclear. It's very dependent on the company because every company has a different business, a different culture, a different org, and a different way of doing things. There's this old Harvard Business Review article from the 90s, which I just love, and I forget the title, but it was something like, "Computers are here, why is no one seeing the productivity impact?" This was a big question, right? It's like, to us, it's obvious: computers make us more productive. This is just incredibly obvious today. But in the 90s, this was not obvious. What was happening was personal computers were being adopted. They were replacing mainframes, and now they were affordable, so the average company and the average startup could buy one. You didn't have to spend millions of dollars on a mainframe anymore. But there was this challenge, and there was this paradox. Companies were adopting them, but they were not seeing productivity improvement. What's going on? The Harvard Business Review article made the case that in order to get a benefit from computers, you have to restructure your entire business process around computers. They have to be at the center of the way that you do things. If you still have paper filing cabinets and a bunch of drawers full of stuff, and it's still a paper and pen kind of physical process, and there's a computer somewhere on the periphery, you're really not going to benefit. But if you throw away your filing cabinets, you throw away your desk drawers full of papers, and you put a computer at the center of it and that's the way that you do all your business processes, then you benefit. There was this split between companies. Some were doing this fairly painful change and they benefited from it, and others didn't. I think it's kind of the same thing now. A lot of companies are trying to figure out how to benefit from the productivity impacts of AI. There's just a lot of experimentation, and everyone is trying different approaches to figure out how to benefit from it. I don't think there's one right approach. Okay. And look, I think that when we see something grow as fast as Claude Code has grown and as fast as Anthropic has grown, it's good to just talk this stuff through, and it's good to hear your perspective. So okay, that's tokenmaxxing. Now, tokens of course are the output of the model, like the words or portions of words that the model outputs, and the words and portions of words that go into it, right? And that is how these companies charge, and the more you have, the more data centers you need, etc., etc. You know, as these models get better, sometimes I wonder whether they're as efficient as they can be. These big models can sometimes do a lot of work, use a lot of tokens, and even if the output is great, people wonder, "Well, is this sort of just driving up token demand where it could have been a really easy process?" And the models are expending many, many tokens and not getting there as efficiently as they could. Let me give you an example. I've been using Claude co-work to make PowerPoint presentations. It's really good at it, and I've been using the Opus 4.7 model. A couple of times I've said, "Alright, you know, you're working on this. Ship it as a PDF." And it just starts to lose its mind. It cycles and it uses as many tools as it possibly can and, you know, it just seems unable to ship the PDF. Eventually I kept telling it, "No, you're making this PowerPoint, you know where it is, ship it," and it goes, "I owe you an apology. I went down a rabbit hole worrying about a constraint that wasn't actually blocking us. The file's there," and then it shipped it. I mean, talk a little bit about the efficiency of these models, and whether that is a legitimate worry that, as we've seen this growth, part of it is these loops that a model like Opus 4.7 might find itself in to do basic tasks. Yeah, generally when we think about models there are a few different aspects of it. One is just how intelligent is it, another is how fast is it, and another is how efficient it is. We generally try to move all these together. Between these, I think we should probably optimize for intelligence. That's the most important thing. Even if it's a little bit less efficient but it's more intelligent and lets you do more things, that's really useful because the efficiency optimization comes after. After we make it more intelligent, then we can make it more efficient. So we sort of do one, then we do the other. We've been experimenting a lot with how exactly we give people control over this because we don't always know the right default. Sometimes when you're using it, you know better. And so one mechanism that we had for this is picking a model. You can pick Opus or Sonnet or Haiku. Right. And Opus is the biggest, Sonnet middle, Haiku smallest. That's right. And this is just the size of the model. Then there's effort. Effort is essentially how much effort you want to put into it. The word is actually really descriptive. You can set this. We have a recommended effort. For example, to maximize intelligence for Opus 4.7, you want to use extra high or maximum effort. But if you want it to use fewer tokens, you can pick medium or low effort. This is a control that you have. Yeah. I talked about this on a show recently and we had a commenter who came in. I was of the opinion that these bigger models will find a way to become more efficient on things like the export-to-PDF task. We had a commenter come in who wrote, "Alex, they can't fix things like that PDF problem. It's inherent to LLM technology and it's the biggest barrier to useful widespread dissemination and usage of agentic AI." I think, trying to translate that, what they were trying to say is we talked about predictions earlier—that this is all probabilistic, it's sort of predicting the next word, you don't get the same answer from an AI agent twice, and so therefore this type of thing is a feature of the way that they work and not fixable. What do you think? Oh, I don't think that's right. Let's zoom out a little bit. Engineers are the first adopters, right? Engineers started using Claude Code like a year and a half ago. This was before non-engineers were using agents in a meaningful way, before co-work and so on. If I think back to what Claude Code was a year and a half ago, it wasn't very good. I could use it to write a little bit of code, but if I really trusted it to build an entire feature or an entire product, it wouldn't turn out well. It did the same thing: it would go in spirals, and the quality was not good, or it built it and either the code was bad or it didn't work. At some point, it just started to get better. As the model improved and as Claude Code improved, the result just got better and better and better. Fast-forward to today, Claude Code is 100% written by Claude Code. Co-work is 100% written by Claude Code. An increasing number of features are fully written by Claude Code across Anthropic and our products. This is something that we hear from customers as well. I did a talk at Y Combinator, the startup incubator, yesterday. I asked people to raise their hands—everyone's using Claude Code—and I asked them, "Raise your hand if 100% of your code is written using Claude Code today." About half the hands went up. Then I asked people, "Raise your hand if 0% of your code is written with AI." There was like one hand that went up in this room of a few hundred people. Power to that person, whatever they're doing. That's right, that's right. And, you know, there's still room for this obviously. Then everyone else was somewhere in the middle, meaning most of their code is written with Claude Code but not all of it. But that's kind of the place where the model is at today. It was not there a year ago. A year ago it was not good enough for this. So this is exactly what you're seeing play out with co-work right now. It's still early; we released it just a few months ago. It's going to keep improving. It's going to keep getting better as the product gets better and as the model gets better. But this is early days. I think everyone using co-work today is still an early adopter. Everyone even using AI today is an early adopter. There are so many people in the world, and most people have not tried AI in a meaningful sense. So there's just a lot more room to improve this. Yeah. We're hosting an event here in San Francisco on June 18th, and a lot of the marketing material I've churned out with co-work. Now, I go back and forth—I don't let it one-shot it, so I'm looking at the copy. But I do things like upload our download statistics to sort of show the growth of the podcast, and I gave it the names of the speakers and it is amazing at building a prospectus: "Here is what the event's going to be, who's going to be in the audience, here's who's speaking, here's why you should be there, here's how to get in touch." Insane. It's so good. What was your feeling like the first time that you used it and the first time that you saw the agents use your tools? Well, I mean, obviously I've sort of enabled everything. I think this is kind of an experience that many people have had where there's a browser extension for Claude and you realize that you can only get the full benefit of this, or you'll get the most benefit, by letting Claude take over your browser and do things for you. The experience is almost the same as I had with a Waymo, where those first couple of turns I was like white-knuckling and watching, thinking, "Should I approve this? Let me read everything." Then you start to trust it a little bit, and you just hit approve, approve, approve, right? In the Waymo, it's the same thing. You're like, "Okay, this looks like it's not going to kill me." And then five minutes later you're on your phone as the AI does the work. That was my experience with code and co-work. Does that sort of track? This is like my experience too. I think it's like any technology. I was watching a friend who has been learning to use co-work over time, and she's not an engineer. There was this use case the other day. There was a language input issue on her computer, where you choose between languages on a laptop, and she couldn't figure out how to fix it. Before, what she would have done is go to Google and ask, "Hey, how do I fix this issue that I'm having with my computer?" This time she just asked co-work. Co-work was like, "Cool, let me take a look. Can I use your computer?" She said yes, and it took over the computer. It gets this kind of orange glow, and you get to watch as co-work opens settings, sees what's going on with the language picker, diagnoses it, and fixes it. You're still in the driver's seat, so you can see this happening and you can monitor it; it's not happening in the background. But it's just magical. My instinct would have been to open Google, so it's funny that for her, she went to using co-work for this. This is actually something I feel all the time. I think for people who have grown up with these products and have seen previous versions, they might not be as ambitious as they could be. But for people who are new to the products, I often see them using Claude Code and co-work for things that I wouldn't have even thought of, and it's just amazing. It's so creative, and I learn a lot every time I see it. Yeah. Now the biggest drawback right now, I would say—and I've seen you reply to people on X about this—is the rate limits. When I see people say, "I've given Claude Code a shot but I'm kind of done with it," it's typically because they've hit their token allotment, and it only works for like an hour for them and then they have to wait four hours to use it again. They start looking for alternatives. What do you think the rate limits have done to the ability for your product to grow, and what is the plan, if there is one, to make people be able to use this without those rate limits? This is something we're actively working on. The reality is a very small percentage of people actually hit their rate limits, which is surprising. For pro users it's a little bit higher; for Max it's actually quite low. I think regarding what you're saying when people talk about it, there are a couple of things happening. One is that we actually reduced the peak rate limits, but that's now rolled back and we've actually doubled those rate limits. We're giving people more rate limits, but there was a brief period where we reduced them, and so people were running into that. The second thing that's happening is Claude Code is actually quite extensible. People can use plugins and all sorts of integrations, and some of these use tokens in a pretty inefficient way. A thing that we've been working on is surfacing this to you so users can decide: do you want to use this plugin or do you not? You can see what percentage of your tokens goes to it. The third thing is there are a lot of people who have increasingly become power users. When we first released Claude Code, you ran one Claude at a time. Nowadays, on my computer, I run maybe five at a time, and then some nights I run hundreds of Claudes at a time, all in parallel. A hundred? Yeah, a hundred, sometimes thousands. This is something that I just wouldn't have imagined a year ago, and obviously this uses a lot of tokens. There are a lot of people figuring out these new workflows that use a lot more tokens, and this is sort of at the edge of what you can do with a Max plan. This is why you can also just pay using the API. If you want to have as many tokens as you need, you can do that too, and this is what a lot of enterprises do. Right. Now, it wasn't long ago where I'm pretty sure Dario, Anthropic's CEO, was referring to OpenAI and talking about the spending on the build-out. Boris, it's great having you here. Like I said, I'm in your product daily, so it's really fun to speak with you about it. We talked a little bit about this, but I think one thing we should highlight is that this is really going to extend beyond the chatbot. We talked about booking flights. I talked about it with marketing presentations. And the week that we're talking, you have a new use case out where Claude co-work can be used for small businesses, including taking over QuickBooks and doing some bookkeeping. Where does this go? What does the broad roadmap look like? We're thinking about a few things for Claude Code and for co-work. There are a few big themes. One is improving intelligence, and I think almost all of this is just the model. As the model improves, we can do more and more ambitious work. For coding, it used to be writing a line of code at a time; now it's building entire features or entire products. For co-work, it started pretty recently, but it was like making a document, and now it's things like booking flights, combining many tools, and doing your QuickBooks. This frontier is improving and moving just very, very quickly. We're also thinking about how to do longer-running tasks. For Claude Code, we recently shipped this thing called Auto Mode. Auto Mode is essentially a replacement for permission prompts. Before, what we used to do is whenever the model used a tool, Claude would ask you, "Is it okay if I use this tool?" Usually you just say yes, and you get kind of tired of saying yes over and over. But it's actually very important for security that you're very thoughtful about this. What we were realizing is that instead of being thoughtful about every prompt, because we're showing people so many of these dialogues, they just got fatigued and they would just say "yes" or "always allow." Auto Mode is the answer, and this is a new way of routing these tool calls. The way that it works is whenever Claude wants to use a tool, it asks another Claude, "Is it safe to use this tool?" Claude has some of the context, but it doesn't have all the context. There's also a number of layers of safety checks, and we spent months iterating on this to make it really safe. There are thousands of different benchmarks and evals that we use to make sure that this is safe. Essentially we found, both in the laboratory setting and now in the wild, that this is safer than what we had before. As a user, it's a really nice benefit because you don't have to sit there and say "yes" over and over. And actually, the result is better because if there's one unsafe command buried somewhere in this big list of things that Claude asks you to do, you might have accidentally said "yes," but if you ask a second Claude using Auto Mode, it's not going to say "yes." This is one big investment. Maybe the third big one is just running more Claudes in parallel. One of the cool things about Claude—and this is something that we started to see pretty early with Claude Code users—is actually very few people nowadays run one Claude Code at a time. Most people run many, many Claude Codes, ranging from a few to thousands. With co-work, we're starting to see the exact same thing. As you get more comfortable letting co-work run, you start a task, and then you start a second task, and you move on, and you do more in parallel. I think there's just a lot of opportunity to make this experience very nice and to make it more obvious for people: how do you do this, and when do you do it? Right. Even if the leverage per person goes up, you still just can't hire enough good people because the demand is so insane and there's so much more to build. That's still the bottleneck for us. But people would argue that if this stuff was so powerful, you could say, "Take a look at the way my sales organization operates, and then configure Salesforce that way with a prompt." Well, we're starting to see—there's one person on the team who was using Claude to do their taxes. I would not necessarily recommend this, but they did that. Yeah, but imagine how much leverage that has—asking the right question. That's true. That's a good point. So, we talked about Salesforce, so we have to talk about the SaaS-pocalypse. You have some interesting views on the type of software companies that will be safe as we get more automated programming and those that might be in trouble. You've talked previously about the different moats that exist, and which moats are more important and which moats are less important. Can you just share that briefly while we're talking about it? There's this really good framework called the Seven Powers for talking about moats in business. There are so many of these frameworks for this, but this is my favorite. I actually studied economics in school; I didn't study computer science. So this is still kind of the way that I think, in terms of these kinds of frameworks. There are a bunch of these different moats. One is scale economies, meaning as you scale up your production, there are increasing returns to scale. Another one is network effects, which is like a messaging app—the more people that are on it, the more valuable it is for any person. Another one is switching costs, and there's another one that's process power. I think most of these moats are still going to matter, and relatively some are going to increase in importance over the next year and some are going to decrease in importance. One that I think will increase in importance is something like network effects, because it doesn't matter who's writing the code. It doesn't matter if it's an agent at the core of your product or something else, or if there's intelligence in your product. If there's a network effect in your product, that's still going to matter. Some moats get less important, and this is, for example, switching costs. Because if you want to switch from vendor A to vendor B, you can—you, you can just ask Claude to do that, and Claude is going to get better and better over time at it. And so I think as a company, a thing that you should be thinking about is: what are your moats? I think a lot of the largest companies just have many, many moats; it's not just one thing. Because the way you get to a scale and the way you build a defensible business over time is you accumulate these moats. You need a number of them. But yeah, I would just think what's going to be more valuable in a year and what's less valuable. I think that when you think about these different software companies, though, if you're using Claude Code, do all moats kind of blend away? Because you could potentially be in this like one app that is interfacing with all software, which means, therefore, there's really only one software company. Yeah, I mean, there's just like a lot of ways that this could play out. I think something like this is possible, but it seems a little bit far-fetched to me. Because if I think about, for example, let's say I'm using a messaging app, how do I decide which app to use? I use the app that my friends are on, that I can reach. So it doesn't matter if I can build a really awesome app for myself, which I can do today—like, I can build a great messaging app with Claude Code in like a few hours—it's still not useful because I can't talk to my friends. But this is the example exactly. You'll have just—you can fact-check me on this—you're going to have an agent in your messaging apps that's going to let you know when your friends have messaged you. I know you use Claude Code on your iPhone a lot, right? So then you will just see the notification and you'll speak it back to people. But yeah, like, I can have an agent that uses my app to do that messaging using an existing app that supports this. So yeah, it's not obvious how it's going to play out. I think today people use a mix of apps and agents. But I do fundamentally think that a lot of these moats are actually still going to increase in value over time. You can think of another example, let's say, like a TSMC or some kind of chip manufacturer. If you think about the amount of work that they put into making a process, and in making a process where the costs go down with scale, this is a fundamental economic force. And there's a lot of companies that do this kind of thing, especially in manufacturing where, with scale, the cost goes down. With tech companies, this is the case for infrastructure. So if you build a really great infrastructure, you can support more users and then the marginal cost per user goes down over time. So if you have this kind of effect, it doesn't matter if you or I can build apps; that's still a really powerful moat. But I do think for sure both things are in play. Okay, I got three more in 10 minutes. Let's see if we can get to them all. Jack Clark, one of the Anthropic founders, recently said, I think, that he believes there's like a 60% chance that these models will start improving themselves by 2028. It could be off by a percentage or a year, but ballpark that's accurate. You're in the app where coding happens autonomously. You're running this app. Do you agree with Jack? Seems right, yeah. When I look at the way that Claude Code is written, 100% of Claude Code is written using Claude Code. This has been the case since, I think, November of last year, since Opus 4.5. It's like a fast takeoff scenario then. Do you anticipate that? I mean, it's possible, and like, this is why Anthropic exists. And so you think, like, this is kind of like a stupid thing—like, how can this possibly lead to intelligence? Like, you teach this thing to predict the next word, and somehow, if the next word is hard enough, it has to learn to really plan ahead and it has to learn how to do all of this. Sometimes I wonder when I see big tech changes underway—and in my career covering this stuff, some have worked out and some haven't—I always have to ask myself: how are we sure that this is the future and this is not a fever dream? But I also wonder, you have to sort of question how much you can extrapolate towards the future in terms of how will this continue to progress. Gavin Baker on Orbital Compute, TSMC, and Frontier Models

Invest Like The Best(66.5K)·82:11·43.6K views·2026-05-20
Patrick O'ShaughnessyHost, Invest Like The Best Gavin BakerManaging Partner & CIO, Atreides Management
TL;DR1.
1. TL;DR The artificial intelligence expansion represents the most extraordinary acceleration of revenue and compute demand in the history of capitalism, driven by unprecedented inflections like Anthropic’s massive ARR growth. However, sustaining this trajectory requires...

1. TL;DR

The artificial intelligence expansion represents the most extraordinary acceleration of revenue and compute demand in the history of capitalism, driven by unprecedented inflections like Anthropic’s massive ARR growth. However, sustaining this trajectory requires overcoming physical constraints in energy ("watts") and silicon manufacturing ("wafers"), forcing a shift toward custom silicon, usage-based enterprise pricing, and highly advanced orbital compute networks.

The Unprecedented Scale of AI Growth & Valuation Dynamics

  • The Anthropic Milestone: Anthropic added approximately $11 billion in ARR in an incredibly short period. To put this in perspective, it took three of the most successful SaaS companies of the last decade—Palantir, Snowflake, and Databricks—ten years of collective operations and tens of thousands of employees to build businesses of that combined scale.
  • Disparities in Capital Efficiency: Anthropic has operated with roughly 80% less capital burn than OpenAI to reach a comparable revenue scale. Consequently, Anthropic is positioned to potentially generate positive cash flow this year, demonstrating a significantly higher structural return on invested capital (ROIC).
  • The Transition to Usage-Based Pricing: AI is rapidly shifting from "all-you-can-eat" flat-rate subscriptions to pay-by-the-drink usage pricing.
      • This shift mirrors the cellular telecom expansion of 2005–2007, where usage-based pricing unlocked massive, compounding revenue curves.
      • Unconstrained by flat-rate boundaries, top-tier labs like OpenAI and Anthropic are positioned to easily clear $200 billion in ARR.
  • Cross-Sectional Valuation Anomalies: Public market valuations currently exhibit massive logical contradictions. Semiconductor capital equipment companies trade at 40x next-quarter annualized earnings, while DRAM (memory) manufacturers trade at mid-single-digit multiples, despite the vertical pricing growth of High-Bandwidth Memory (HBM).

Solving the Compute Bottleneck: Watts and Wafers

  • The "Watts" Bottleneck & Zoning: Near-term energy availability is constrained more by zoning, approvals, and turbine casting limits than by grid capacity. However, capitalism is rapidly adapting, with massive capital expenditure projects coming online to expand grid infrastructure and repurpose alternative energy sources by 2027–2028.
  • The Case for Orbital Compute: Long-term energy limitations will inevitably be resolved by migrating inference compute into space.
      • Racks in Space: Orbital compute is not characterized by giant, floating space stations, but by standard server racks (such as a 3,000-pound Nvidia Blackwell rack) operating as individual satellites in sun-synchronous orbits.
      • Thermal and Power Efficiency: In sun-synchronous orbits, 500-foot-long solar panels remain constantly illuminated, while massive radiators extend into perpetual shade.
      • Inter-Rack Connectivity: Racks are linked into virtual data centers using vacuum-traveling lasers, building upon the laser-communication architectures SpaceX already deploys at scale on Starlink V3 satellites.
  • TSMC as a Bubble Preventer: Taiwan Semiconductor Manufacturing Company (TSMC) single-handedly prevents a speculative hyper-bubble by strictly limiting wafer supply. If TSMC expanded capacity to meet unconstrained demand, Nvidia could potentially sell $2 trillion to $3 trillion in GPUs by 2026/2027, leading to a catastrophic overbuild.
  • The Texas "TeraFab" Initiative: A joint venture involving Elon Musk, Tesla, and SpaceX aims to build the world’s largest fabrication facility in America. Partnering with Intel to access 50 years of institutional manufacturing knowledge, the project leverages Musk's ability to construct hardware facilities in a fraction of standard timelines (such as building the Memphis supercluster in 122 days).

The Chip Design Battlefield: Prefill, Decode, and Hardware Lifespans

  • The Iron Triangle of Chip Design: Similar to military tank design (balancing attack, defense, and mobility), chip architects must negotiate rigid physical trade-offs between memory capacity, memory bandwidth, and compute power within TSMC's manufacturing rules.
  • Prefill vs. Decode Disaggregation: Modern model architectures allow designers to optimize chips by separating these two distinct phases:
      • Prefill (Loading the Cannon): The process of ingest-loading the prompt and context, which is heavily memory-capacity bound.
      • Decode (Firing the Cannon): The step-by-step generation of output tokens, which is memory-bandwidth constrained.
  • Cerebras and Wafer-Scale Computing: Chip startups cannot succeed by trying to build a "better GPU" because Nvidia maintains insurmountable scale and supply chain advantages. Startups must do something fundamentally different and physically difficult. Cerebras achieved this through wafer-scale engine architecture, keeping massive compute and memory on a single giant silicon wafer to bypass traditional chip-to-chip packaging bottlenecks.
  • Salvaging GPU Capital and Private Credit: The disaggregation of prefill and inference tasks allows older GPUs (like Nvidia Hopper or Ampere architectures) to remain highly productive. By placing ultra-fast inference processors (like Cerebras or Grok LPUs) in front of older GPUs to handle prefill, operators can extend the useful life of legacy silicon to 10–15 years, securing the underlying assets of the massive GPU private credit market.

The Application Layer Crisis and Geopolitical Realities

  • Trillions in Value Destroyed: At the application layer, AI has actually net-destroyed trillions of dollars of enterprise software value. Shallow SaaS applications have struggled to adapt as foundational frontier models commoditize basic software workflows, though highly specialized coding tools (such as Cursor and Cognition) remain strong exceptions.
  • The New Prisoner's Dilemma of Frontier Labs: Model builders face a critical strategic choice: do they release their state-of-the-art weights via open APIs, or keep them entirely closed?
      • Releasing them open-source allows competitors—particularly resource-constrained Chinese firms—to rapidly "distill" and clone the models using cheap reasoning traces.
      • If the top Western labs collectively agree to stop releasing frontier APIs, they can effectively halt the rapid catch-up of open-source competitors.
  • Geopolitical and Battlefield AI Dominance: The war in Ukraine has demonstrated that battlefield superiority is no longer governed solely by physical hardware or drone counts, but by the sophistication of real-time targeting and orchestration AI. This algorithmic edge is shifting the global balance of power, potentially ushering in a new era of Pax Americana driven by Western computational dominance.

And if you are on that $250 or $300 or $280 a month plan or whatever it is, you are getting severely rate limited. You are getting a lobotomized version of the AI. Because like we talked about, Claude now produces 70% less tokens. I was a telecom analyst in '05 to '07, and cellular had been a great growth industry really for the last 10 years, and the reason was you had a combination of fixed pricing—you had 900 minutes for whatever it was—and then usage-based pricing over that. And when did cellular stop being a great growth industry? When everybody just went to all-you-can-eat. And, by the way, long distance was the same thing. Now, we have a crude variant of continual learning today when something is verifiable, and that's just, you know, reinforcement learning during mid-training. But, yeah, continual learning is a model that dynamically adjusts its weights, or adjusts in some way in real-time. Like, as a human, what's what you do. Yeah, like if I, the first time I touch, or, you know, put my hand in a fire, I've learned, I never put it in there before. The iron triangle of tank design is that all designers of a tank, they have to make tradeoffs between attack, defense, and mobility, and you know, for obvious reasons. You know, the disaggregation of prefill and inference really have opened the aperture for making these different tradeoffs, because you can make very aggressive tradeoffs for decode, aggressive tradeoffs for prefill. Prefill being taking in the context, decode being, you know, write the output. Yeah. I have a great colleague named Andrew Fox who said, "Picture, you know, a British naval ship from the 18th century. Prefill is loading the cannon, decode is firing it." And what prefill literally is is just the model understanding the question, the prompt, and then kind of keeping track of its own decode, of its own answer, and that is fundamentally a memory capacity-bound problem. Decode is the process of generating new tokens, and that is memory bandwidth-constrained. And so if you are a chip designer, this gives you a richer canvas to paint on. But even so, it needs to be hard, because if you make different tradeoffs in that iron triangle to optimize for memory capacity, and they're not hard tradeoffs to make, well then, Nvidia is going to make those same tradeoffs, they get better prices from Taiwan Semi than you're ever going to get, and good luck. Global Keynote: The Beginning of Better | SAP Sapphire Madrid 2026

SAP(152.0K)·93:32·599 views·2026-05-20
Christian KleinCEO, SAP
TL;DRSAP Sapphire Madrid 2026 marks SAP's pivot from a traditional enterprise software provider to a Business AI company.
TL;DR SAP Sapphire Madrid 2026 marks SAP's pivot from a traditional enterprise software provider to a Business AI company. By merging its 50-year ERP database with generative AI, SAP has launched the Business AI Platform and the Autonomous Suite, deploying 224 specialized...

TL;DR

SAP Sapphire Madrid 2026 marks SAP's pivot from a traditional enterprise software provider to a Business AI company. By merging its 50-year ERP database with generative AI, SAP has launched the Business AI Platform and the Autonomous Suite, deploying 224 specialized agents and 51 assistants designed to execute mission-critical business processes with 100% accuracy.

The SAP Business AI Platform: Build, Reason, and Govern

To move beyond the limitations of public LLMs—which lack corporate context and data privacy frameworks—SAP has introduced the Business AI Platform to serve as the technological backbone of the autonomous enterprise.

  • The Rich Context Layer: Infuses generative AI with the internal "brain" of the ERP, mapping processes across over 7 million data fields using a custom Knowledge Graph.
      • SAP Domain Models: Trained directly on SAP’s proprietary code to interpret complex, customer-specific business rules.
  • Joule Studio 2.0: An open, agnostic development environment that lets users build multi-agent workflows using SAP or third-party LLMs.
      • Developer Incentives: SAP is offering Joule Studio 2.0 and its runtime environment free of charge until the end of 2026, alongside a €100 million investment to accelerate partner ecosystem development.
      • n8n Workflow Integration: Workflow automation platform n8n is deeply integrated into Joule Studio 2.0, allowing users to connect SAP data to hundreds of external, non-SAP systems without writing code.
  • Unified Business Data Cloud (BDC): Acts as a semantic data fabric across multi-cloud environments.
      • Strategic Acquisitions: SAP announced its intention to acquire Dremio (a high-performance, federated data lakehouse supporting open formats like Apache Iceberg) and Prior Labs (a frontier AI lab for tabular foundation models like TabPFN).
      • Tabular Foundation Models: The new SAP-RPT-1.5 model offers high-accuracy tabular predictions and a Chat API for real-time "what-if" simulations and predictive business forecasting.
  • Enterprise-Grade Governance: Built directly on SAP LeanIX, the new AI Agent Hub (launching Q3 2026) acts as a central command center to catalog, monitor, and manage the compliance of SAP and non-SAP agents.

The Autonomous Suite and Joule Work

SAP is transforming its legacy systems of execution into an Autonomous Suite spanning five core functional domains: Finance, Spend, Supply Chain, Human Capital Management (HCM), and Customer Experience (CX).

  • Multi-Agent Orchestration: Launching with 224 out-of-the-box agents and 51 assistants that coordinate autonomously to complete fragmented enterprise tasks.
  • Joule Work & "Spaces": Replaces traditional transactional screens with an "app-less" zero-UI experience. Context-driven, transactional, and analytical screens dynamically generate on-the-fly to facilitate human-in-the-loop approvals.
  • Company Memory: Built on SAP Signavio, this context graph captures unstructured "tribal knowledge" (PDFs, Teams messages, emails, process models) and refines them into "process atoms" that instruct agents on corporate policies and historical exception-handling.
  • Audit-Ready Compliance: Developed in coordination with external auditing firms, SAP’s finance-focused agents operate under a SOX-compliant, ISO-certified framework to guarantee trace-level auditability.
  • Hybrid Support via RISE: For enterprises mid-migration, SAP is enabling a significant portion of Joule Assistants to run on hybrid landscapes, including S/4HANA on-premise and legacy ECC environments.

Industry AI and Real-World Value Realization

SAP is deploying tailored Industry AI solutions across 26 sectors to deliver concrete business outcomes rather than disjointed software packages.

  • Deloitte Partnership: Deloitte has built 78+ industry-specific agents on SAP's platform. Internally, thousands of Deloitte consultants use *Joule for Consultants*, realizing up to a 30% reduction in time spent on daily tasks.
  • H&M Group Digital Transformation: Leveraging RISE with SAP, BDC, and SuccessFactors, H&M co-developed:
      • Store Intelligence Agent: Scans real-time market signals (e.g., local events, localized demand surges) to proactively recommend next-best-actions, trending apparel features, and store floor configurations.
      • Store Concierge Agent: Blends online and in-store customer experiences to deliver real-time stock availability and highly personalized outfit recommendations.
  • Automated Asset Management (RWE): Multi-agent processes diagnose turbine defects, prep technician dispatch, and deliver real-time onsite manuals, aiming to decrease unplanned wind-farm downtime by 15%.
  • Revenue Growth Management (Coca-Cola HBC): Automates complex settlement reconciliation processes across 29 countries, auto-resolving standard distribution exceptions and surfacing root-cause analyses for disputes.

Strategic Sovereignty and Technology Alliances

To serve regulated industries, SAP is emphasizing absolute security boundaries and regional data compliance.

  • Mistral AI and Cohere Partnerships:
      • The Mistral AI platform (featuring Le Chat and AI Studio) and Cohere North are now generally available on SAP Cloud Infrastructure within sovereign European data centers, offering a 100% European-hosted AI stack.
  • Nvidia OpenShell Integration: SAP is integrating Nvidia's open-source OpenShell framework to secure enterprise AI at scale. OpenShell guarantees that agents operate strictly within permitted data boundaries, use authorized tools, and generate fully traceable execution paths.

Notable Quotes

"If agents run your payroll, your financial close, do your demand and supply chain planning, 80% accuracy is just not good enough. When you run the world's most mission-critical business processes, your agents should not guess." — *Christian Klein, CEO of SAP*
"Soon every company will have a workforce of agents. These specialized agents will not replace enterprise software. They will make enterprise software more powerful than ever." — *Jensen Huang, CEO of Nvidia*
"By partnering together, we are bringing world-class European-built models... and SAP is bringing what no one else can do: deep business context. The processes, the data, the workflows that are actually running the companies." — *Arthur Mensch, CEO of Mistral AI*
"Great software adapts to the person, not just the task." — *Sebastian Steinhaeuser, Chief Operating Officer of SAP*

Next up, we can allow for predictions on the fly, because the opportunity in Business AI was never just large language models; it was AI built for decision intelligence, reasoning over your structured data stored in tables. Think of predictions like a demand forecast in retail or predictive maintenance in asset management. So far, we still had to rely on specialized AI models, or what are sometimes called narrow AI models—one for each task. Now, if you take ten predictive tasks in the enterprise across ten company codes, you would easily have to train 100 narrow AI models. This is months of work for your data scientists, whom you oftentimes don't even have. It's a significant investment, and it's hard to scale and adapt. So we thought: Can we not put all of that into one giant model? With the release of SAP-RPT-1 last year, which is the most performing tabular foundation model on SAP data, today we are very excited about the next model generation, RPT-1.5. So we built all of these assistants and we made extensibility a first-class principle, but we realized there was still something missing—something big: your company context. The organizational memory that sits in the minds of your people, who have executed these processes for years and years and years. This knowledge, often referred to as decision traces, often sits as unstructured data in process models, policies, PDFs, even chats, and long, long emails. Who doesn't know them? This makes it effectively invisible to any agent on any agent platform. We have built something to address this tribal knowledge gap, something we call Company Memory. Company Memory is a knowledge management layer, or you can call it a context graph for your company. Specifically, it is built on the same foundation as SAP Signavio, so it is already natively aware of your process models and your process insights. You can additionally feed it your policies and procedures, relevant content from Teams or Slack channels, and even long approval emails or exception chains from which it can extract what we call process atoms, which are small, structured units of knowledge. Each one can guide an agent on what to look for, what to do, and most importantly, what not to do. When an agent makes a call, it also maintains traces for full transparency and control, and when it runs into an exception, it adds to the company memory how it dealt with that exception. You, of course, have the power to also directly update a rule, or review a role or process manually. Once done, all agents adapt to it instantly. This is what allows us to bring the best of public LLMs, SAP's unique context layer, and your own company context together to deliver results you can rely on. Palmer Luckey Wants America to Win

Hoover Institution(1.2M)·53:47·40.4K views·2026-05-20
Palmer LuckeyFounder, Anduril Industries
TL;DRTo counter growing global threats, the United States must transition from "world police" to a "world gun store" by arming allies with cheap, autonomous, and rapidly deployable defense systems.
TL;DR To counter growing global threats, the United States must transition from "world police" to a "world gun store" by arming allies with cheap, autonomous, and rapidly deployable defense systems. To outpace China, America must abandon bloated, slow "cost-plus" defense...

TL;DR

To counter growing global threats, the United States must transition from "world police" to a "world gun store" by arming allies with cheap, autonomous, and rapidly deployable defense systems. To outpace China, America must abandon bloated, slow "cost-plus" defense contracting, aggressively reclaim its physical manufacturing base, and reform a patent system that currently serves as a blueprint for foreign adversaries.

***

From "World Police" to "World Gun Store"

  • A Shift in Strategic Doctrine: The US no longer possesses the domestic political will to sustain long, multi-trillion-dollar "boots-on-the-ground" foreign wars. Instead of acting as global police, the US must act as a supplier.
      • Equipping Willing Allies: The US should focus on arming nations that are willing to fight for themselves (e.g., Japan, Taiwan, Germany) rather than sending American troops to defend nations that lack the domestic will to self-defend.
  • Competing as a Global Supplier: To function effectively as the "world's gun store," the US military-industrial complex must operate like a competitive commercial enterprise.
      • This requires keeping inventory in stock, meeting delivery deadlines, and recognizing that sovereign buyers have alternative purchasing options in Russia and China.

Defense Products vs. Cost-Plus Contracting

  • The "Cost-Plus" Incentive Trap: Traditional defense "primes" (e.g., Lockheed Martin, RTX, Boeing) rely heavily on government-funded "cost-plus" contracts, which financially reward companies for delays, inefficiencies, and budget overruns.
  • The "Defense Product" Model: Anduril operates as a defense *product* company rather than a traditional contractor.
      • Private R&D Investment: Anduril uses its own capital to develop products and reinvests 100% of its revenue back into internal research and development.
      • High Margins on Cheaper Hardware: By producing hardware that is faster and significantly cheaper to manufacture, Anduril achieves up to a 40% profit margin on mature products while selling them to the government for a fraction of the competitors' cost.
  • Case Study: The YFQ-44 Fury: Anduril invested $900 million of its own money to develop the US Air Force's first autonomous fighter jet, beating out traditional aerospace giants.
      • The aircraft progressed from contract signing to its first flight in just 556 days—making it the fastest clean-sheet jet fighter development program in modern history.
  • Limits of the Product Model: Private speculation cannot fund every defense asset. Extremely high-cost capital projects (such as nuclear aircraft carriers or bioweapon defense research) still require direct government backing and oversight.

The China Challenge and Hollowed-Out US Manufacturing

  • Unbalanced Purchasing Power: While the US defense budget sits around $900 billion compared to China’s estimated $400–$500 billion, China achieves up to a 10x return on every dollar spent due to domestic supply chains and highly automated manufacturing.
  • The Loss of Real Engineering: The US has decoupled innovation from physical manufacturing, turning American engineers into "architecture astronauts" who layout high-level designs but rely on Chinese engineers to solve actual manufacturing, electrical, and mechanical problems.
      • Raw Materials and Supply Chains: True technological sovereignty requires control over raw feedstocks. Relying on China for critical tech hardware component production undermines US national security.
  • Unified Military Purpose: China has structured its military with a single, highly focused objective: invading and occupying Taiwan. By contrast, the US military must build and maintain a force capable of fighting across 2 to 2.5 diverse global fronts simultaneously.

Patent Reform, Capital Markets, and AI Warfare

  • Patents as Adversarial Blueprints: The current Western patent database acts as an open-source instruction manual for China. Because patents require public disclosure, foreign adversaries can immediately copy technologies while Western competitors must wait 20 years for patent expiration.
      • National Security Patents: Luckey advocates for a massive expansion of "classified patents"—allowing companies (like Google in the field of AI) to secure intellectual property rights without disclosing highly sensitive technical details to the global public.
  • US Capital Markets as a Strategic Weapon: Unlike the highly centralized, state-subsidized Chinese tech sector, US venture capital operates as a vital playground for high-risk, rapid innovation.
  • Demystifying AI on the Battlefield: AI used for military targeting and piloting is highly deterministic, verifiable, and rule-bound—unlike conversational "large language models" (LLMs) that hallucinate. Military AI is designed to reliably identify specific, un-ignorable signatures (e.g., distinguishing a Chinese destroyer from a fishing boat).

Silicon Valley's Cultural Split and Anduril's Patriotism

  • The Tech-Military Divorce: A distinct cultural rift emerged in Silicon Valley in the early 2010s, with multinational tech workers frequently protesting military projects (such as Google employees forcing the company to cancel Pentagon contracts).
      • Major tech companies often brand themselves as "transnational" entities rather than American companies, partly for overseas tax sheltering and partly due to a globalist corporate culture.
  • Anduril's Counter-Culture:
      • Veteran Integration: Service veterans make up 15% to 20% of Anduril’s 7,000-person workforce, anchoring the company’s product designs in real-world operational needs.
      • Forward Deployments: Unlike companies that keep staff remote, Anduril routinely sends engineers directly to active conflict zones (including Ukraine and the Middle East) to iterate on hardware features in the field alongside combat operators.

***

Notable Quotes

"The United States has a history of fighting for other countries that maybe aren't very interested in fighting for themselves... It doesn't make sense for us to send our people to go die for a country or a form of government or anything that's not directly aligned with US interest if they're not willing to die for themselves." — Palmer Luckey
"We're not teaching engineers how to be engineers anymore. We're not teaching designers how to actually design things to be manufactured; we're teaching them how to be high-level design shops that put together design packages that get sent to the real engineers in China and they actually figure out how to do the work." — Palmer Luckey
"Patents are Chinese instruction manuals... Western companies patent things so that they can trade temporary exclusivity on an idea in exchange for eventually it enters the public domain. And all that really means, in practice, is that... China can just rip it off right away and Western companies can only rip it off after 20 years." — Palmer Luckey
He wears Hawaiian shirts and tells the Pentagon how to defend the Republic. Palmer Luckey on Uncommon Knowledge, now. Welcome to Uncommon Knowledge, recording today at a Pacific Research event here at the Reagan Library in Simi Valley, California. I'm Peter Robinson. A native of Long Beach, Palmer Luckey was homeschooled by his mother. He attended Long Beach State and dropped out to develop a virtual reality company. In 2014, Mr. Luckey sold that company, Oculus, to Facebook for $2 billion. He was 21. In 2017, Mr. Luckey founded Anduril. "Palmer, thank you for driving up from El Segundo for us." "It was—it was no problem at all. No, I took my helicopter partway, actually. I had another engagement." "Palmer Luckey on 60 Minutes just a year ago, I'm quoting you, Palmer: 'I've always said that the United States needs to transition from being the world police to being the world gun store.' What do you mean?" "Well, there's a lot packed in there. The first one is the United States has a history of fighting for other countries that maybe aren't very interested in fighting for themselves. Maybe there are times that we want to go in there for U.S. interests, but generally speaking, it doesn't make sense for us to send our people to go die for a country, or a form of government, or anything that's not directly aligned with U.S. interests if they're not willing to die for themselves. And I think, for example, you saw our withdrawal from Afghanistan prove this out. You know, the moment that we weren't there, it turns out that the people there didn't really care much to maintain what we had been saying was what they wanted the whole time. And so, if you're the world police, you're out there trying to—trying to fight that battle for these countries. I think we need to transition into a role where we are equipping these countries with what they need to defend themselves. If they want to fight for themselves, if they want to defend themselves, we should help them do that. And then, I think that the gun store analogy works in a few other ways. If you're going to be the world's gun store, you need to do things that a store would generally do to stay in business. You have to deliver on time, you have to keep things in stock, and you cannot—you cannot necessarily pretend that you're the only store in town because we aren't anymore. Countries have options in China, they have options in Russia, and we need to recognize that and not pretend like we're—like we're the only store in the entire world that these guys can buy from. And then, I'd say the last bit here in terms of world police versus world gun store: you can disagree with me, and you can think my strategy is wrong. You can think that actually we should maintain a tight grip on all these weapons, that we shouldn't sell to the Japanese and let them do their own thing, we shouldn't sell to the Germans and let them do their own thing—look at how it went last time. Those are reasonable critiques, but I think the reality is the United States does not have in us, politically, another large-scale boots-on-the-ground war to fight for somebody else. I don't even think we have it in us for a good cause. What I had said in that interview actually was, 'I don't think we have another D-Day in us, even for a just cause,' because Americans, particularly people of my generation and the generation before, they've seen this play out. They've seen that these things turn into long, multi-trillion-dollar slogs where the benefits are very, very diffused. And so you can go and say, 'Hey guys, this is a really great cause,' and they say, 'You know, a lot of things start out as a great cause, and—and pretty soon it's been 20 years and you've spent $5 trillion and your highways are still broken and nobody has the jobs that they wanted.' And so, I think realistically we have to take a different approach." "Anduril, the story of Anduril. When you co-founded Anduril in 2017, you were a 20-something with plenty of cash. You could have enjoyed yourself. You could have gone into consumer electronics—you love video games. Not too far from here is Electronic Arts, which is a very big business; you could have gone into that. You founded Anduril instead. Why?" "There's a lot of—there's a lot of reasons, but a lot of people don't necessarily know this: before I started Oculus, when I was a teenager—and because I lied about my age—I was working in an army-funded research center on the Bravemind program, which was using virtual reality exposure therapy to treat veterans with PTSD. And that was where I was first exposed to how big of a difference technology could make to the people that are in our military. It's what exposed me to how bad our procurement system was, how so much was done on a cost-plus basis where you made more money if it took you longer, you used more stuff, and it was more expensive. It also exposed me to the fact that nobody, not in academia and not in industry, was interested in figuring out how to cut production times in half or how to cut product prices in half. Because if you cut the production time in half, then, well, where's—where's your 'plus' going to come from on all that cost? If you make something that's $6 million into a $3 million thing, you're literally just losing money. And so I realized that was a horrible, broken set of incentives. It wasn't until I had money from Oculus that I felt like there was something that I could help do to solve that. At the same time, I had moved up to Silicon Valley and I had spent several years there running the virtual reality division at—at Facebook after they acquired my company. To make a long story short, there was a national divorce going on in the early 2010s between our tech industry and our national security apparatus, even in Silicon Valley, which was started by funding for Department of Defense projects. And that's a very dangerous experiment. We've never run that experiment in this country, where you've had that national divorce. We've always had our most innovative people, our most innovative thinkers, and our most creative technologists working on national security problems. What happens when that's not the case? What happens when these companies refuse to work with our military? And so you have to ask yourself: you've got all these companies that are divorced from national security and they don't believe in the basics of what they are actually saying. They're saying, 'Oh, you know, people deserve a right to have their own identity, why are you erasing these people? I refuse to work with you guys—unless you're Taiwanese, and then we'll—we'll—we'll throw you under the rug in—in a second.' I realized that's a really dangerous situation for our country to be in. And so I wanted to start Anduril to save taxpayers hundreds of billions of dollars, to get people out of these advertising companies and these entertainment companies that are controlled by foreign adversaries, and to put them to work on the tools that are going to hold those adversaries at bay." "Thank you. Give us just a moment or two on the difference between the way Anduril operates—you've touched on this, but I want to clarify—the difference between the way Anduril operates and the way the so-called primes operate: Lockheed Martin, RTX, Boeing, and the others." "Look, these companies are building some cool products, and they have a long history. So I'm not here to say that they're—that they're awful, and in fact, I work with most of them, but there's a big difference between us. Anduril is not a defense contractor; we're a defense product company. And the key word there is 'product company.' We use our own money to decide what products to build, how to build them, and then we build those products, and then we sell them to the government. It is a model that exists across more or less the entire United States economy; this is how almost everything you buy is—is built. And there are very few examples otherwise. One industry where cost-plus is still common outside of defense is residential renovation. And—and I have to look out into this—into this world: how many people out there who have done a renovation on a cost-plus contract were satisfied with the incentive structure and the outcome? Of course you weren't, right? You know, it goes longer, and the contractor makes more money. The GC is asking all his subs to boost their prices and then, 'Oh, I'm going to give you a nice little kickback on the side,' where the money comes back to you, but because the—you know, the invoice is bigger, I get to charge the government a bigger 6% on top. It's all the same shit. It is identical. And so, the difference there is we make more money when we make our products cheaper. We make more money when we make them faster. When I deliver a year ahead of time, I don't have to fire half my team because there's no money to pay them; in fact, I've just saved an enormous amount of money, and I probably get to promote those people and have them work on some other product. And then, the other big difference is we take all of our money and put it back into research and development—every single penny. So, a lot of other defense companies are putting about 1% to 3% of their revenue back into internal research and development, and much of that is cost-matched by the government. We are putting 100% of our revenue back into it. Now, you can say, 'Palmer, that's not going to hold up, you won't be able to convince your investors to do that forever,' to which I would say, 'I've pulled it off for eight years and change.' I've got a clear roadmap to doing it for about another four or five years, and I think I can stretch it out a lot longer than that. And the nice thing about U.S. capital markets: if you're growing fast, if you are growing, people will let you get away with a lot. Look how long Jeff Bezos's investors let him get away with Amazon net-not making money. If you have a healthy business and you could turn that switch, people are not going to demand it. So look, someday are we going to have to start returning money to—you know, things other than R&D? Yes, but for now our customers like that when they give us money, they know that it's either going to a product or into their next product." "Okay, so I just want to stay with that point for one moment. A billion in revenues in 2024, up to about 4.3 billion is what I found as I was doing research for this year—that's about the projection for this year. And then a valuation on that four-three..." "And those are just public projects." "Okay, all right. What I'm getting at is..." "We have showrooms. We have one with our public products and then we have another one that's government-only."
