Two questions. One: how do we keep them from stealing innovations?" "Stop patenting everything. Patents are Chinese instruction manuals. You're taking your most valuable stuff, you are—look, so the founding fathers never predicted a world where you would have a globalized economy where the entire patent office could be downloaded every single morning and then ripped off and then used to fight a war against you. The—the problem that we have right now is that Western companies patent things so that they can trade temporary exclusivity on an idea in exchange for eventually it entering the public domain. And all that really means, in practice, is that for the 20 years or so between when you file for a patent and when somebody could launch a product that is a rip-off, it means China can just rip it off right away, and Western companies can only rip it off after 20 years. And then you repeat this cycle over and over again for every single generation. And it's killing us. And so, I don't—I hardly get patents anymore. There's a few things here and there, mostly defensively, because unfortunately, our patent system allows people to sue you when they think that you're—they're infringing. We need to really, fundamentally revisit the patent system. And I'm not just complaining here; I've got a possible solution. I think we need to massively expand the national security patent process. You can—you can obtain a classified patent. You can get a patent on something that you are not allowed to disclose to anyone, but you still maintain the exclusivity on those rights. We need to massively expand that program and also massively expand all the categories of things that are covered by mandatory national security disclosure laws. In—during the Cold War, a lot of sensors and microcomputer technology was covered by this because the government said, 'Oh my god, if the Russians get this, it's over.' And we put in all the money, we put in all the time, and they're just going to be able to rip it off. If we let them rip all this stuff without paying any of the money to get there, then all of their money will go way further than ours, where we're doing the fundamentals of all of this. I think, for example, that a lot of the things Google patents around artificial intelligence—they are of national security importance. We can't require, you know, security clearance for every engineer to work on these things, but I'm—I'm suggesting something where it's much broader but the bar is lowered quite a bit. "And what about our capital markets? Is that our big—there's no Peter Thiel in China." "No, well, I mean, look, Peter—I mean, Peter's a great example. You know, where did I get—where I got into it? "In general. Like, basically, the way that I look at autonomy is it's a thing that enables you to make the right decisions in certain cases. It—like, I'm not—there's going to be ships that are fully autonomous, no people aboard. What's going to be much more common are going to be ships that are very lightly manned because most of the jobs have been automated on them. In other words, an aircraft carrier that doesn't have 5,000 people aboard—it has 120. A destroyer that, instead of having 1,000 people aboard, has a crew of 50. Automating a lot of these jobs and using a combination of robotics and automation and—and just materials that don't, for example, require so much constant anti-corrosion treatment, right? We—we could build a Navy where, like, I'm not saying that autonomous ships are the answer, but autonomy integrated everywhere is the only way we're going to be able to do it. Because if we have a Navy that is, let's say, three times as big in terms of ships, we do not have enough people to have three times as many people in the Navy. It's just never going to work. And—and the worst part is when you build a new ship, you don't just build a ship and then let it sit and wait for war. You have to fill it with people and you basically have them play—play pretend continuously, so that they're ready to fight. They know how their ships work. So, you need to sail all over the place, you need to do exercises. It costs almost as much to do nothing as it does to fight a war in some cases. With autonomy, you might be able to change that equation. I could build a thousand autonomous ships, or mostly autonomous ships, and I could put them in dry dock or I could wrap them in a big, giant cellophane wrapper and fill them with argon gas. I call this concept the Twinkie ship concept—it's a—you—you put it in a wrapper and it lasts for a thousand years. And if you could do that, you might be able to make, like—the cost of building the Navy is not building the ship, it's maintaining and sustaining and then replacing that ship when it wears out. If we can just make the cost of building a ship the cost of building a ship, and then you wait for war to come, it's a game-changer. So, I—I think 10 years, we need to be thinking about how we can do automated shipyard production, building a Navy that has the same or even fewer personnel—more specialized, higher-paid, even more—more skilled in their particular disciplines—and then we need to have three or four or five times as many ships." "Okay, let's talk about the question of resources, which is tied to the question of national will, with which you began earlier. Well, it's worth noting too that with that 4% of GDP, remember that Trump is out there, and Marco Rubio is out there demanding that our allies spend 5% of GDP on national security. But in the same way you go to war with the Navy you have, you write budgets with the weapons that you can actually fund, right? Like, this hypothetical that I have of this—like, this future Twinkie ship Navy, I mean, we need to build stuff right now to stop really major threats. We need to refill our magazines that we've just depleted, firing everything out of them. And would it be awesome to say, 'Well, what if we could build a replacement for the Tomahawk that wasn't designed in the '60s and it was more manufacturable and we're going to buy a whole bunch of...' okay, that's great, but we do also need the Tomahawk given that a lot of our doctrine is built around it. Our ships' launchers are built around it, everyone's trained to—to use it, and our—our allies like Japan are also trained to use it. And so, when people say things like, 'Well, why don't we just replace it with a cheaper system?' Okay, well, it's going to take a few years to do that, and it's going to take a few years to get it out there. How long is it going to take to train people to use them? To find all of the problems? To work out all the bugs? And so, I—I think that Trump is reacting to the world as it exists today, and realistically, if we're going to continue to have the same warfare... Like, when we talk about, you know, a 1.5 trillion budget, I—I mean, you could spend that money just basically getting our military back up to standard with the weapons that we already have. We have tons of weapons that are corroded, they are depleted, they are out of service, and our ammunition stockpiles—I'm not just talking about missiles, I'm talking about even just normal, smaller ammunition—are hugely depleted. The point is, there's a lot of money to spend to overcome a lot of the rot. I hope that we can get a $1.5 trillion budget this year, and I hope that 10 years from now, it's a fraction of that." "Got it. All right, very nice. A few last questions. Here's the late Henry Kissinger. He's writing this just a couple of years before his death: 'If you imagine a war between China and the United States, you have artificial intelligence weapons. You can't tell exactly what will happen when AI fighter planes on both sides interact, so you are then in a world of potentially total destructiveness.' Close quote. Now, you are warm and funny and articulate—you must be a bear to work for, but when you're on a stage at least, you're warm and funny. You wear Hawaiian shirts, but there is at a minimum a kind of free-floating anxiety about AI, which is a large part of your enterprise, and here we have Henry Kissinger, he co-wrote this book with Eric Schmidt, former CEO of Google. There are serious people who say we should be scared to death of the likes of Palmer Luckey." "It's very reasonable. It's—it's very—it's very reasonable when you put it that way. Oh, there was a—there was a—there was a quote—a quote I'm trying to remember. What was it? Give me just a second, there—I had—it's, you know, it's on the tip of—it's—it's—it's on the tip of..." "...your brain. You're too young to have that problem." "No, I—I'm—I've got too much on my brain to not have that problem. And so, you know, imagine if you had asked Kissinger about his opinion of the useful memory limits of a home computer, you know, he would have probably had an answer that was even worse. No—no offense to him, but when he says you have two fighter jets that go up against each other and you don't really know what's going to happen, like, I fundamentally disagree with that premise. You can build formally verifiable software that responds in a very consistent and deterministic way. I can make something that responds the exact same way every single time, perfectly, even based on AI systems. And—and—and the—the problem is, he's looking at probably large language models, you know, like, he's looking at ChatGPT and saying, 'Oh, they're non-deterministic and they can do these random, weird things, they hallucinate.' The types of AI you build for doing large language models are radically different from what is used for flying a fighter jet—radically different from, for example, determining the difference between a fishing boat and a Chinese destroyer. And people say things to me like this all the time: 'Palmer, it might not know the difference, what if it makes a mistake?' I—I promise you, when you look at the thermal signature, the visible outline, the electromagnetic emissions, the wave profile, the—like, the wake profile of—of—of a destroyer, you cannot mistake it for anything. You—you—I'd be willing to bet my life on every single detection of that destroyer that it is not a fishing boat. And a lot of people who are not experts in this area, who aren't working with it, they—they—they imagine it in the more interesting, free-form way that Hollywood has taught them to. They see this thing as a Terminator problem. They see AI as a lot more open and sentient and less subservient to man than—than it actually is. Maybe someday we're going to have a totally different type of AI, like, imagine, you know, you have the true sentient, thinking, conscious machine that looks out for its own interest, it's capable of self-evolving, self-teaching, you know, as John Connor said, 'You don't understand, it'll never stop, it can never die, it never sleeps, it never grows tired, and it won't stop until you're dead.' We are a long way away from that form of—of AI, and I'm not saying I'm not afraid it might happen someday, but if you rank the things that I'm afraid of, it's way down the list. I'm afraid of bioweapons that are tailored to kill particular ethnic lines, or even family lines. I'm—I'm afraid of people using AI to develop bacteria that will, you know, destroy a lot of our agricultural reserves. There's so many things I'm afraid of, and I'd say if anyone is really afraid of these things, they should actually talk to the people in the military who are tasked with developing the doctrine around these things. Because if you don't believe me, you don't trust me—you shouldn't. Take everything I say with a pound of salt, right? I'm the guy making billions of dollars, I'm the guy with the pitch deck that says I want to make tens of billions of dollars, selling arms for taxpayer money. Don't trust me." You should talk to the people who are actually tasked with deploying these weapon systems and ask them what they think. And if Kissinger went up to the people who are doing this—for example, the Experimental Operations Unit, the EOU in the United States Air Force, which is currently operating our FQ-44 autonomous fighter jets today, and they are flying them, developing doctrine for what a future war might look like— If you asked, if Kissinger said, "But if these two fighters meet, you don't even know what's going to happen, really, do you? It could be total war." I mean, they they they they they they, I think, they might be polite to him in the room, but you wouldn't believe what they'd have to say after he walks out of it. And I think those are the people we need to listen to. Last question, and I'm going to take a moment to set this up, because again it strikes me as basic. In 2004, the late political scientist Samuel Huntington wrote an essay about a new class of Americans that he called Davos men. Huntington, quote: "These transnationalists have little need for national loyalty, view national boundaries as obstacles that thankfully are vanishing, and see national governments as residues from the past whose only useful function is to facilitate the global elite's global operations." Close quote. For the first time in history, in other words, the United States of America had produced a class of people who had no use for the country itself. And I have to confess that when I looked around Silicon Valley, I thought at the time that Huntington was correct. Mark Zuckerberg gives a commencement address at Harvard, in which he praises, quote, "the forces of global community." Close quote. Bringing the world closer together. There we go. In 2018, Google employees forced the company to drop a contract with the Pentagon. By the way, just last week Google employees did it again. Talk—while we're speaking of Google—more than, I'm quoting a news account from last week, last week: "More than 600 Google workers signed an open letter to the CEO expressing concerns about negotiations between Google and the Pentagon." Close quote. Davos men, internationalists, globalists. And along come Peter Thiel and Mark Andreessen and you. Palmer Luckey, why are you such a patriot? Well, the good news is, you know, that's a really intelligent and articulate way to put it, but you don't have to be that intelligent or that articulate to understand the problem. I think a lot of underst—the people, Americans, understand the problem. They're all members of of of the mono-party or uni-party here in the US and abroad, and they buy into a certain set of political values and social values they largely didn't decide for themselves. They're isolated from the consequences of those decisions. You know, I I saw a lot of this when I was in Silicon Valley. I don't want to beat up too much on Mark Zuckerberg, you know, for, you know, learning Chinese or asking, you know, asking the Chinese to help him with everything, but, you know, I I got to go back to my time when I was there. There were a bunch of flags that flew on the Facebook campus. You had a pirate flag, an Instagram flag, a gay pride flag, a a world peace flag, there was a Black Lives Matter, you know, fist for a while. No American flag. And that was because if you asked them at the time, "Are you an American company?", the official answer—and not not, this is not me making it up, this is what they testified under oath to Congress—they said, "We are a—we are an international organization that transcends borders." And the main reason for that is that they were headquartered for tax purposes in Dublin, Ireland, and so if you say that you're an American company, then the gig is up. But, but, so it's a little unfair, it was really a tax, but but culturally it was true, right? They thought of themselves as part of, you know, this Davos elite type person, and even, you know, the individual programmers thought of themselves that way. By the way, half the people at Facebook weren't born in America. And I'm not saying that that there's something bad about being an immigrant, but when you have a place where most of the people did not grow up in a country with its values, you're going to end up inherently with a place that much more greatly centers its care outside of the United States and prioritizes things outside of the United States. I mean, you might remember the Claude constitution at Anthropic, that its publicly posted constitution was that it it consider all answers from a perspective that does not unduly weigh Western perspectives or values. I—it's explicitly being told to to to to not value the things that the West has decided are important around, you know, self-governance, freedom of association, freedom of speech, that we mostly still have and the Europeans are forgetting about. But anyway, you asked me, you know, what what I think about this idea. The reality is, we all know who these people are, and I think the first people to figure it out were not the intelligentsia. I think it was actually blue-collar workers in, you know, working working in Detroit, Michigan. They understand way better than the people who go to Davos do. Pfizer CEO: How AI Will Reshape the Future of Medicine | Podcast | In Good Company

Norges Bank Investment Management(117.0K)·47:20·3.9K views·2026-05-20
Albert BourlaCEO, Pfizer Nicolai TangenCEO, Norges Bank Investment Management
TL;DRUnder CEO Albert Bourla, Pfizer is pivoting post-pandemic by investing heavily in oncology and obesity therapeutics, driving organizational agility through decentralized AI, and adapting to compete with China’s rapidly accelerating, low-cost biotech ecosystem.
TL;DR Under CEO Albert Bourla, Pfizer is pivoting post-pandemic by investing heavily in oncology and obesity therapeutics, driving organizational agility through decentralized AI, and adapting to compete with China’s rapidly accelerating, low-cost biotech ecosystem. ...

TL;DR

Under CEO Albert Bourla, Pfizer is pivoting post-pandemic by investing heavily in oncology and obesity therapeutics, driving organizational agility through decentralized AI, and adapting to compete with China’s rapidly accelerating, low-cost biotech ecosystem.

Post-COVID Pivot and Strategic Acquisitions

  • Portfolio Rationalization: Pfizer deliberately divested its consumer healthcare and off-patent generic divisions to focus strictly on high-risk, high-reward innovative medicine.
      • Albert Bourla (Chairman and CEO of Pfizer) emphasizes that a corporation's role is to excel at a few things, whereas hedging is the role of the investor.
  • The Post-Pandemic Financial Cliff: Pfizer experienced a massive revenue drop from its COVID-19 portfolio, falling from $56 billion in 2022 to just $6 billion in 2023. This drop challenged internal confidence but validated the company's focus on long-term resilience.
  • The Seagen Acquisition ($43 Billion+): Pfizer acquired Seagen to gain a dominant position in Antibody-Drug Conjugates (ADCs), a highly promising class of cancer drugs.
      • ADC Precision Technology: Bourla compares ADCs to "extremely precise missiles" where an antibody acts as a biological GPS to target cancer cells, and the payload (the drug) acts as a warhead, sparing healthy tissue.
  • The Metsera Acquisition (Obesity Portfolio): Following scientific setbacks that forced the cancellation of internal assets due to liver toxicity, Pfizer acquired Metsera to secure a competitive foothold in the obesity space.
      • Competitive Outlook: Phase 2 clinical data confirmed Metsera's potential to deliver a monthly dosing regimen, which would offer a major advantage over the weekly products currently marketed by Eli Lilly and Novo Nordisk.

Optimizing R&D and Clinical Success Rates

  • Outperforming Industry Success Rates: Pfizer's clinical success rate is 18–19%, nearly double the pharmaceutical industry average of 10%.
      • Improved R&D Metrics: Under restructured governance, Phase 2 clinical success rates jumped from under 20% to over 50%, and regulatory approval rates now sit near 95%.
  • Eliminating Operational Silos: To fix past issues where Pfizer successfully developed molecules but miscalculated their commercial viability, Bourla unified early-stage discovery and late-stage development under a single end-to-end R&D leader.
  • A Culture of Organizational Confidence: To build self-confidence within a changing organization, Bourla actively highlights and scales up localized successes rather than letting the corporate focus dwell solely on failures.

The AI Revolution and Decentralized Transformation

  • The Five-Year Impact: While Bourla is skeptical of claims that AI will cure all diseases in 15 years, he expects AI to fundamentally reshape the standard of care, drug target discovery, and manufacturing by 2030.
  • Machine Learning in Practice: Pfizer's COVID-19 antiviral pill, Paxlovid, was one of the first highly impactful global molecules discovered using machine learning.
  • Decentralized AI Governance: Pfizer rejected a centralized AI model in favor of giving decentralized business units (manufacturing, research, commercial) direct accountability and resources to build their own AI use cases.
  • Fostering AI Literacy: To overcome employee fear of displacement, Pfizer launched voluntary training programs for thousands of workers and is implementing internal AI fluency certifications.

Geopolitical Competition: China’s Rise in Biotech

  • Scientific Dominance: In the 2025 Nature Index rankings for scientific output, eight of the top ten global universities were Chinese, with Harvard being the sole US institution in the top ten.
  • China’s Biotech Infrastructure: Through meticulous five-year planning, China has built a competent regulatory body modeled on the US FDA, established strong intellectual property (IP) protection, and created a fast-track judiciary system to resolve patent disputes.
  • The Competitiveness Gap: Chinese biotech firms operate at half the cost and three times the speed of Western pharma giants. Bourla warns that Western companies have a five-year window to transform through technology before China begins scaling its own drugs globally.
  • Western Policy Recommendation: Bourla advises Western governments to stop spending 80% of their energy trying to slow China down, which he views as a waste of resources. Instead, they must focus 80% of their efforts on improving domestic R&D speed, university funding, and regulatory efficiency.

Leadership, Corporate Culture, and Personal Heritage

  • Culture Over Strategy: Bourla believes that culture is more important than leadership and strategy because a strong culture elevates the performance of all employees, turning average performers into high achievers.
  • 360-Degree Feedback System: In 2019, Pfizer implemented a biannual evaluation system where supervisors are rated entirely by their peers and direct reports on decisiveness, vision, and communication.
  • Managing Executive Influence: Recognizing that his passionate Mediterranean style can occasionally stifle open debate, Bourla uses structured "rounds" in meetings to ensure all executives speak before he shares his own conclusions.
  • Personal Heritage of Resilience: Bourla credits his unrelenting optimism to his mother, a Holocaust survivor who narrowly escaped a firing squad in Greece. Her survival instilled in him a lifelong belief that "nothing is impossible" and a focus on opportunities over setbacks.

Notable Quotes

"I’m a believer that a corporation's role is not to hedge. It is to be very good at a few things. Investors' role is to hedge." — Albert Bourla, Chairman and CEO of Pfizer
"No one follows a pessimist... Pessimists are usually right. But there is nothing great that has happened in humanity without an optimist behind it. Nothing." — Albert Bourla, Chairman and CEO of Pfizer
"[The US] should stop trying, with all the heart and brains that they have, to put 80% of their effort into how to slow down China. Very big mistake... We should put 80% of our focus and effort into how to become better than China." — Albert Bourla, Chairman and CEO of Pfizer
Hi everyone, and welcome to In Good Company. I'm Nicolai Tangen, the head of the Norwegian Sovereign Wealth Fund. Today, I'm in particularly good company because I'm here with Albert Bourla, who is the chairman and CEO of Pfizer, one of the largest pharma companies in the world. Albert has been with Pfizer for 30 years, has been CEO since 2019, and has led the company through a remarkable period of change. Warm welcome! Thank you very much. And you know, I've been with Pfizer for 33 years. I've been with my wife for 30. All right, I got it mixed up there. Yes, yes. Now, a lot of stuff has happened since you took over as CEO. What are you most excited about right now? Yeah, I think I'm always excited by the future, like all CEOs, right? They see the opportunities ahead. Science is at the highest levels we have ever had it, and science is affecting our business in a very big way. So, I'm very, very optimistic about the scientific progress that we can see in the next years until the end of the decade. You sharpened the focus on innovative medicine, sold off consumer healthcare, and so on. Why did you think that was the right decision to make? I'm a believer that a corporation's role is not to hedge. It is to be very good at a few things. Investors' role is to hedge. I believe that requires a different culture for a scientific company, a different culture for a consumer company, and a very different culture for an off-patent, generic company. So, I felt that we should choose to be good at what is really important, and that was science. Now, we're not going to spend much time on COVID, but we have to just touch on the fact that you did an unbelievable job, put a huge amount of your own capital in there, and produced a vaccine in eight months. Just how was that possible? Well, you know, it was possible for several reasons, but the most important was that these were exceptional times. Everybody felt that this was not business as usual, and everybody felt that the culture of society—our civilization—was at risk. So, everybody gave everything. And when I say everybody, I mean the government, our people, and our hospitals that collaborated. It would not have been easy to do without everyone being aligned. Why don't we continue to work this way at full speed? Because we don't have that big need driving people's behaviors, and they have the tendency to go back to the old habits. I have seen that with regulators; after being extremely instrumental in helping achieve what we achieved, they went back to being more conservative. I have seen that with government operations that were opening the ways for us to be able to accomplish what we accomplished, and now they are back to the normal trend. Unfortunately, you need a common enemy to motivate people. Absolutely. Yes, we see that in many places. Now, on the back of that success, you had a big pile of money, and then you had to decide what to do with it. How do you decide on a thing like that? First, let me tell you that I don't think it was only the money that came to us that was a very big asset. The most important thing was the belief in our organization that there is nothing we can't do. Our people felt so proud about what they were able to accomplish. As a company and as individuals, we were showered with awards and recognition that made us all feel that when we put our wills together, we can bend any curve. That is a very big asset, and I think we found it extremely helpful once things did not go well—after COVID was controlled by what we did and, as a result, the money stopped coming in as fast as it was. The fact that we had developed this resilient culture helped us a lot to pivot to something new. Now, the money was quite, quite good, and we invested it all. There were a lot of people, of course, who had different views as to how this money needed to be invested. There were conservative people who would say "give it as a dividend." There were people who were saying "buy back shares"—and, you know, our shares were at 50 at that time. There were also people saying "it's time to invest in the business," so it was a unique opportunity. You have an LOI period, so you need to make sure you make investments. I chose the last path. We did a significant amount of investments, almost 80-plus billion dollars in acquisitions. Three of these make up 80% of the total cost. Still, the jury is out, particularly on the biggest one of them, which was Seagen. It had products that were already on the market, and over there, it is an overwhelming victory. Those products are doing tremendously well. We'll come back to those, but first, that kind of confidence you gain by achieving something like you did during COVID—how long does that confidence last? It was a very tough period in the year 2023. In '22, our revenues from COVID were 56 billion dollars. Now, it is 6. So, it was a huge drop. But the confidence—I mean the fact that people think they are superhuman. I'm coming to that. When you are having such a drop in reality, from the stock being the best performing to suddenly the stock being the worst performing, that puts a lot of doubt in everyone's minds, collectively and individually. That included me; I was wondering if I was a failure just months after I was the hero of the world. But also, I knew that included many people in my organization because they perceived the stock price as an external validation of success. When that was not there anymore—and, of course, not the weekly recognitions we were receiving—they felt very bad. Still, because we had developed this resilience and because this memory was lasting a lot, I believe we were able to overcome it. In '24, we came up really with a winner's mentality, with confidence that we can go and win in the marketplace, we can win in business development, and we can win in research. That is what happened in '24 and '25. They were exceptional years in terms of performance. Everybody was very impressed by how we were able to grow the top line, beat analysts, and reduce costs. Organizational confidence is such an interesting thing, I think. One of my favorite movies is Kung Fu Panda. I don't know—you probably haven't seen it because it's a children's movie, but basically, they're trying to figure out... I love children's movies. They're trying to figure out why this panda is so strong, and what his magic thing is, and they find these magic ingredients—and it's basically just a mirror. He just believes in himself. How do you foster organizational confidence, self-confidence? It comes with confirmation through success. It is very difficult to build a culture of confidence if people see only failures in front of them. You need to take it together with successes, and you need to magnify them. There are always pockets in the organization that have examples of success and pockets that don't, and usually, everyone's attention is on what went wrong. You need to make sure that you project to the collective organization what went right as well. Let's go back to the acquisition. You spent more than 40 billion to buy Seagen. What was it that you saw there that other people didn't see? It was one technology, to be frank, and that technology was ADCs. It was a technology that was starting to become one of the hottest new things in oncology. It was not yet at that time, but it became much bigger. There were a lot of singular players here and there that could have ADC technology, but there was only one that had four products on the market and 13 R&D projects in the clinic. ADC being antibody-drug conjugates. Antibody-drug conjugate. It is one of the latest and most promising ways to fight cancer. Why does it work so well? You should consider ADCs like those extremely precise missiles that modern warfare is using. Why are they so extremely precise? Because they have GPS systems that take them directly to the target, and then, based on what the target is, they can adjust the warhead. They can make it big, small, or explosive. This is exactly how ADCs work. The antibody is the GPS, and it is programmed biologically to identify a location in the body, go there, and stick to it. The drug is the warhead carried together with the antibody, and once it hits the target, it releases the impact. Imagine a chemotherapy that doesn't go everywhere, but goes predominantly to the cancer cells. That's the ADC technology. You can change the antibody, and you can send it from the liver to the brain, to the bones, to the stomach. You can change the payload, and you can use radioactive payloads, chemotherapy, protease, or a lot of different weapons. That's exactly the beauty of it. From a scientific and business perspective, it gives you an opportunity to have so many combinations and so many shots on goal. That's why I really believed in that, and we did it. If the technology is proven to deliver on the promise, it is going to be one of the most successful acquisitions. If the technology doesn't deliver what we were hoping it would do, I think we will have a modest return, but I don't think we will lose money. So, that was in oncology. Now, the other area you wanted to zoom in on was obesity, and you bought Metsera. Why was that so important to buy? And you beat Novo Nordisk there. Yes. We always believed that obesity is a very big area, and we always believed that Pfizer is the company that can be very competitive there. We entered into the obesity space way back in the day. If things hadn't gone wrong for us scientifically, because two major assets we had in the clinic suddenly showed signs of liver toxicity, we had to discontinue them, although they were very effective. But if that had not been the case, we would be the first to launch an oral product right now in the market, and things would be very, very different for all of us. Unfortunately, this didn't happen. So now, we have a development organization that knows how to do primary care—very big, complicated studies. We have a commercial organization that is a powerhouse and knows how to sell stuff. We have a manufacturing network that can make this stuff better, faster, and cheaper than anybody else, and guess what? We didn't have a portfolio. It was extremely important for us to cover this gap because of scientific bad luck with an acquisition, and that's what we did with Metsera. Now, you've owned it for a little while. Has your view changed? Not at all, not at all. I think I'm more and more optimistic. If anything... Why are you more optimistic? Because we had the release of data post-acquisition. We knew that Metsera had a very good weekly product; we were very certain—you can never be 100%, but we were 85 to 90% certain—that that would deliver. But we didn't have hard evidence that a monthly product could be delivered. We knew that the pharmacokinetics supported something like that. We knew from Phase 1 early data that it should work, but we needed the Phase 2 data, which we didn't have available when we did the acquisition. The Phase 2 came later, and indeed, it confirmed that we do have a monthly product. We can debate whether it is going to be better than Eli Lilly's or worse than Eli Lilly's, but it's going to be monthly. Eli Lilly's and Novo's will be weekly. That we know now with a very high level of confidence—to the degree, of course, that science can provide. So, that's why. Why is your clinical success rate better than the competition? I think the success rate in the industry is something like 10 to 11%. You are quite a lot higher than that. Why are you so good? I think because you can have this type of success, and you're right: one in ten succeeds in the industry, while almost two in ten succeed in our hands. So, we have an 18 to 19% clinical success rate. We have a very, very long history as a research organization. Actually, Pfizer's research organization is, I believe, one of the best. I'm sure we will discuss what we have done to change the trajectory of Pfizer's organization, but... But why is it one of the best? Because when it comes to capabilities, all the metrics indicate that we can kill it. You mentioned one of those metrics. I can also mention to you the Phase 2 success rates. Where the industry is around 25 to 30%, and we were below that at 15 to 20%, we jumped it to 50-plus percent. In regulatory success, we are almost at 95%. So, all the metrics are showing that we are very good. But I wouldn't say the success is just because sometimes success is because you choose to do easy things and not challenging things, right? Not scientifically big, big projects. If you see the number of breakthrough designations that we receive from the FDA, which is a recognition that this potential medicine is so good that they will put it on a fast track because if it is successful, it can change the trajectory of a disease, we were among the top ones. If you see how many approvals we received, we were among the top ones. So, I think we were always very, very good when it comes to capabilities, but there is a big "but." I don't think we were perceived as very good, for a good reason. Because when you measure how much we put into the system and how much we got out of the system in terms of dollars, we were mediocre—not top quartile, even though in everything else I told you, we were top quartile. So, what did you change last year? What was the reason for that? It became very clear to me that we made the wrong choices in choosing which products to develop. They were not easy technically, which makes me even more angry; they were technically challenging, but we miscalculated the commercial potential of those products. There were silos between R&D and commercial that created that. There was no focus in R&D; whatever we had as positive data, we would go and develop it. That is the number one thing that I aimed to change. The good news is that if it were a question of capabilities for us, that would mean a five-, six-, or seven-year journey to be able to turn that around. If it is a question of focus, with good capabilities but wrong focus, that is a cultural, governance, and leadership issue that you can change within months. So, you got a new head of R&D. What are the other changes you made specifically? We have multiple heads of R&D units who are new, and they bring a different culture into the organization that I wanted, particularly regarding focus. As I said, I was not looking for a better oncologist or a better endocrinologist. I was looking for an endocrinologist who can focus. I was looking for an oncologist who can focus. Those were the types of changes that we made. We put together the entire R&D organization as an end-to-end system, which was not the case before. We used to have early and late separated, and then in late R&D, we also separated oncology. Now, we have early and late under one leadership. This makes a very big difference because the handovers between early and late, when the two organizations are separate, can be difficult. Early means someone discovers the molecule, and once he discovers it, he gives it to the late organization. Late means we have clinicians who do experiments to prove that the molecule works. This handover of "here is my baby, now make sure that it is successful" is one of the most challenging phases in drug development and discovery. I think that's what we did quite well. Let's spend some time on AI. We recently—you and I—spent some time at a conference together where some people think that within 10 to 15 years, we can get rid of all the illnesses in the world with more use of AI. What are your reflections when you hear that? I think that it is a very bold statement that probably exaggerates to make a point, but I think the point is still valid. I think we will see tremendous, tremendous progress due to AI in 10 to 15 years, and even earlier—by the end of this decade, in five years—in addition to advancements in biology. So, that's the point. I don't think in 15 years all diseases will be cured, but I think that in five years, we will have a very different standard of care. How will that change the pharma industry? It will change not only the pharma industry; it will change the health system. It will change hospitals, it will change physicians and how they are organized, it will change regulators and how they see products, and it will change pharma. Pharma will change in research and development because I think we can identify better targets, synthesize better molecules, and have better clinical trials. It will also change manufacturing, and it will make a huge difference in commercial approaches as well. Is it good news or bad news for pharma? I think it's very good news, but we need to be careful because that will be a radical change. That will be a disruption, and disruption always creates new winners and new losers. The bigger the disruption, the bigger the impact. And how will you make sure that Pfizer is on the right side of that change? That's all I sleep, go to bed, and wake up with in my mind. There are three things: China, AI, and politics, unfortunately. Let's do AI first. Specifically, how has it changed the way you operate? Because it was instrumental in helping you with the COVID vaccine, right? Yeah, it was, but not with the vaccine that much. It was with the treatment, Paxlovid. The pill that we discovered was indeed the first machine learning molecule with such an impact, at least that I'm aware of. AI cannot do everything right now, but it can do way more than what we are using it for. That's the fundamental question I'm asking. All these models can do way more than what they are used for, and the question is: why are they not used as much? Right now, we have reached a level where it is not a question of technology; it is a question of organizational ability to adjust and transform itself through AI. That poses a question: what is the best way to enable leaders to transform their organizations with AI? I think the first step is that they should receive the accountability of doing it. There are two views on how to organize AI. One is centralized, where you have visionary AI guys who come in and build your discipline, and then they cascade it to everybody else. The other view is very decentralized. It says that the center is only the infrastructure, the data, the computing power, and the cloud, but the accountability, responsibility, and resources to create use cases lie with the units—with manufacturing, research, and commercial. I chose that approach, and we discussed it a lot at Pfizer. We moved into giving accountability to our leaders to make those changes. They have to deliver, so they have the obligation to execute transformation plans that will really move the needle and go to something very different. I think that's a very, very important thing. How do you close that technology overhang—the fact that these models can do so much more than we are able to utilize them for? I think that's what I was trying, maybe not successfully, to explain: the reason they are not using them is because there is inertia and there is fear. There is fear for one's job, and there is also fear because people don't understand it. There is a lack of AI literacy. But what's the key to solving this? Because it's not only in the pharma industry; it's across society, right? Yeah. The key to solving it is to understand it. If you understand that people are scared because they don't understand it, you need to create AI literacy. How do you do that? We train them. We are putting thousands of people into programs so they will understand how to interact with an AI model. We haven't made it mandatory right now because we have about 3,000 to 4,000 people who have gone through the program. What we will have in Pfizer are certifications. We will have people who can receive a certificate if they are AI fluent, if they are AI very good, or if they are AI experts. We are working very, very intensively to develop all the specifications of what this will include, and we are moving into that very rapidly. What kind of classification would Albert Bourla have? You know, it's interesting. I need to go and pass the tests for all of that. I did the training that the 2,000 people did, and I saw a huge difference in my ability. It was a very easy one, over a weekend, right? I saw... I think it has to be... we have it mandatory in our company, because the people who need it most are least keen to do the training, right? So, I think it has to be mandatory. In the beginning, everybody protested because nobody likes mandatory, but now they... well, my interpretation is that they like it. Very good. Good learning. You mentioned AI and China. What is happening in China in the pharma industry? I think the Chinese, when they put something in their mind as a top priority, have proven they can deliver exceptional results. The Chinese work with a five-year plan. They had the 14th five-year plan in 2025, continuing from Mao Zedong's time. This is where they first spoke about wanting to develop global players in the pharmaceutical space by the years '35 to '36. To do that, they said in the first years they need to develop biotech, improve science, and so on. They did tremendously well. Right now, in the Nature Index of ranking universities based on scientific outputs, the top 10 was always historically eight US universities—the usual suspects like MIT and Harvard. In '25, only one was from the US, which was Harvard, and eight were Chinese. Eight of the top 10 in the world were Chinese, based on peer-reviewed publications they had, grants, PhDs, and all of that. The progress that is happening is amazing, from the early stages of drug discovery to regulations, IP protection, utilizing talent, encouraging investments, and subsidies. They are moving with the speed of light. Is it basic research, you think, which is the main reason behind this? What are the factors influencing this? The factors that influenced it were the execution of a meticulously well-thought-out plan. The first step was that you need to have a regulator, like their FDA, that is not corrupt, is competent, and can differentiate between garbage and diamonds when it comes to science. They did that meticulously well. They changed their FDA regulations after doing repeated visits to the FDA here in the US to learn how they are doing it. The second is that they know that if someone invests their money to make something in biotech, the whole asset is a formula on a piece of paper. It's not the machines, it's not the buildings, and it's not the drills. To protect that, you need to have very strong IP protection. So, they developed a very strong intellectual property protection system in China. This gave confidence to investors that the regulator will be fair, objective, and focused on science, and that patents will be protected. Because of this new law that came, they created a judiciary system that can resolve IP disputes very quickly, so if someone challenges your patent, you don't have uncertainty for 10 years. Do they have a better system than we have? Apparently, they have better results than what we have. Are they ahead of us on oncology, for instance? They are not ahead of us yet, but when you see the curve, you come to the conclusion that it is a question of one or two years to become better than us. I don't think there are many things we can do right now to stop that. So, within one or two years, China will be ahead of us? In multiple areas. Which areas? They are focusing on early stages right now because they don't have global clinical development teams yet, and they don't have commercial development teams yet. So, on the early stage—which is chemistry, preclinical development of molecules, and testing them in areas like cancer and obesity—for every one thing we have, they have ten companies working on it. I'm sure that in the early stages, they will be better and more productive than us within two years. Then, it will come to the point where they need to transition to being able to take those molecules and bring them to the world alone, rather than together with us. I think that will be their second step. But so far, they do it with us, and with you, right? So, you did a deal with 3SBio, which is a Chinese biotech company. Yes. We are trying to tap into Chinese innovation. There are a lot of things they are developing. We look at them, and when we find something good, we license it. But for me, this is not a long-term strategy. First of all, they won't give their molecules to others forever. They will develop their own capabilities to take them all the way to the market, so they can profit from the whole value chain. My focus is on how to become better than them, because right now, they are in a very different league. They are doing everything with half the cost and three times the speed. If you compare Pfizer, Lilly, AstraZeneca, and Novo Nordisk, one is better in something, the other is better in something else, but in general, we are all within a range of the same productivity levels. In R&D, maybe one is better; in commercial, maybe another is better. But Chinese companies come in with half the cost and three times the speed. That's a very different league. You need to transform your companies, and you have five years to do it so that you will be able to compete in a world where they come in like they did with electric vehicles, solar panels, and batteries. They will do the same with medicines. And how is Pfizer going to halve the costs and increase the speed by 300%? The best answer, or the simple answer, is through transformation via technology. I think it is a good thing to say "I'm going to transform myself," but it's not that you were stupid before. It was just that you didn't have technologies that would allow you to do things very differently than how you are doing them right now. Now we do, with AI. So, transformation through technology is pretty much number one. Culture, leadership, and focus—all of these are extremely important as well. But now, it is high time to change the way we do things. And what can regulators and society do? Are there any... if you were to change three things that would make your life easier? I'll tell you what the US should do. It should stop trying, with all the heart and brains it has, to put 80% of its effort into how to slow down China. That is a very big mistake because it's a waste of resources and brainpower—you will never slow them down. The genie is out of the bottle. We should put 80% of our focus and effort into how to become better than China. For that, I know what Pfizer needs to do, and I know what the US government could do to help us. Such as? I have a very comprehensive plan that covers regulatory changes, NIH funding, how universities operate, and drug pricing. All this debate about drug pricing drove 2025 to have the lowest multiples in the history of the industry and the lowest available funds for biotech. Without changing those things, we won't have enough investment there. China represents scale, speed, and cost. We should not underestimate scale; they are putting a lot of money out there. In our case, we are withholding money from this sphere because investors do not feel confident that those products will sell at prices that will give them a return on investment. Albert, let's move on to leadership. Why are you a good leader? I'm not sure if I'm a good leader, but I'm a leader who changes every year. I'm a leader who learns, and one of the key characteristics of a good manager is that time works on him like wine: it makes him better. The moment I feel at the end of the year that I was exactly the same as I was in the beginning of the year, I think it will be time for me to retire. So, Parker rates wines from 1 to 20. What kind of wine rating do you give yourself? Yes, I would say that one of the... 20 is the highest, by the way. Good to know. One of the key characteristics of a leader is humility, so it would be a very bad demonstration of judgment to try to rate myself. But I'll tell you something: I am judged by my people every six months. In Pfizer, we have a system where everybody gets a score every six months about specific leadership traits, like: Is he creating a good environment? Is he thinking big? Is he making decisions fast? Is he changing his decisions? Is he focusing the organization? These are very specific questions, and it has been the same for years now. In '19, I put that system in place when I became CEO. The people who tell if you are a good leader or not represents the exact reverse of before. Previously, we had leadership scores where supervisors would say "he's good, he's not good, he's bad." We did the exact opposite. Now, the people who are working for you and the peers who are working with you will tell if you are good or bad. So, imagine a span of control of ten at Pfizer; that means every person will have ten people who report to him and ten peers in the same team evaluate every six months how he is doing—for example, in being decisive. What are the parameters where you are trying to improve yourself? I know very well because I'm part of this, and the feedback I get is very consistent. I'm Mediterranean. I am more passionate. Yeah, I got a hug from you when you came in here. That was nice. You know, are you like a hugger? If you hug, people appreciate it. If you shout or yell, they don't appreciate it. Many times, I do that. If you compare me to how I was 10 years ago when I was younger and more dynamic, I have shown significant improvement, and compared to 20 years ago, even more. But... But which part are you trying to improve? To be less passionate when I express my opinion, because the higher up you are, the more you can shut down people. I want to create an environment where everybody will speak. If I let it go... for example, when I have meetings, I don't just ask what people think; I go "What do you think? What do you think?" I call it a round. Yeah. Many times, I say, "Okay, let's have a round." When the debate is in a position where things are not concluding, I say, "Let's do a round." Where do you score highest? What is your super strength as a leader? In "thinking big," I get very, very good scores. In "measuring outcomes," I get very close. On being "decisive," I get very good scores. In "speaking up," I get very good scores. It's very clear that you take no bullshit. I like it. It's good. How has your leadership style developed over the years? I developed the softer parts of managerial skills, and I also developed a big admiration for culture. I believe that in a company, you need the right structure, the right strategy, the right leadership, and the right culture. But the area that most companies spend the most time discussing is organizational structure. While important, it is the fourth most important because it is even more important to have the right strategy. If you don't have the right strategy, no matter how you organize your R&D, you will not be successful. But even more important than strategy is having the right leaders. The right leaders—and I'm sure you know this better than anybody else—can take a mediocre strategy and make it brilliant, while bad leaders can take a brilliant strategy and deliver nothing. So, leadership is probably near the top. There is only one thing that is more impactful than leadership, and that is culture. Do you know why? Because at an organizational level, you can't have all the thousands of people in the organization be triple-A players. You will have A-plus, A, A-minus, B, and C players. But if you have the right culture, it uplifts everyone. The C players become C-plus, the B players become B-plus, and the A players become triple-A. I'm a big believer that culture eats strategy for lunch. How is your corporate culture impacted by the fact that you are situated in Manhattan? I think you started in Brooklyn, right? Very much so. Pfizer was founded in Brooklyn 177 years ago. We never left the city. We came to Manhattan 30 or 40 years ago, so we have been more than 100-plus years in Manhattan. Manhattan is a very unique place: multicultural, the Big Apple, and extremely competitive because of Wall Street, the banks, and the lawyers. They are the most competitive people on earth. That created a culture in Pfizer where, regardless of whether you are sitting in Istanbul, Islamabad, Paris, or Berlin, the Pfizer culture is very competitive. So, let's say I join Pfizer—if I'm lucky enough to get a job there—and I work there for a little while. How do I feel that kind of "Brooklyn is still part of this, and this is a bit like a Wolf of Wall Street environment"? People who join Pfizer tell me that they find the company moving very, very fast. If they are joining from biotecks, they say, "We didn't expect you to move as fast as or faster than a biotech." If they are moving from another corporation, they say, "We didn't expect that a large corporation could move that fast." That speed is Wall Street. They also tell me, "Wow, very competitive." People really care about winning; it is not okay to lose. That is something you need to be very careful with in a company because if this competitiveness is used to stop others, it is very bad—the whole organization will go to hell. But if this competitiveness is used to say, "I'm going to be the first one to bring a COVID vaccine to the market," this is what gives you the drive. I am planning meticulously to instill that second type of competitiveness, which is about winning for the patients and winning for the research programs. To do that, we need to work together rather than create individualists. And where does your drive come from? My drive comes probably from my younger age. I think most people develop those traits when they are kids all the way to college. And if you were to be a bit of a shrink on yourself and look at where you came from? My mom. My mom was... Tell me about your mom. My mom was a Holocaust survivor. She was in hiding as a teenager, she was arrested, and she was abused physically, sexually—anything you can think of. She was put in front of a firing squad. At the last moment, a BMW motorbike came and removed her and another person from the line. That was because a Christian relative paid everything they had in bribes to the Germans at that time to spare her life. She talked a lot about her experiences in captivity, but she never talked to me with the spirit of "they did that to us." She never spoke with hatred. I never saw her hate Germans, Nazis, or anyone else—never, never. They were simply irrelevant to her. What she focused on most was the celebration of life, and the belief that everything is possible—that there is nothing you can't do in life. She would say, "I was in front of a firing squad, and I survived." I remember when I would go to her as a child and complain that "I can't do my homework because it's too much," or "someone said something to me at school and I don't want to go tomorrow," I can see her waving her finger in front of me, saying, "Don't tell me you can't do it. I survived a firing squad; go and do it." That was my mom, and she made me very optimistic. She made me look not at what went wrong as a reason to give up, but at what opportunity came out of what went wrong. In her mind, her opportunity was that she had me and my sister. That was the miracle of life. Thank you for sharing that. Tying that back to the beginning, you said a CEO needs to be optimistic. Just how optimistic does a CEO need to be? At some stage, don't you become utopian or a bit too much? I'm a big believer in that. I think no one follows a pessimist, and if there is one thing that is not negotiable for a CEO, it is that he must have followers. If you can't get people to say, "Yes, let's go with him," you can't be a CEO. Now, the truth is that pessimists are usually right. But nothing great has ever happened in humanity without an optimist behind it—nothing. You won't find a single thing where pessimists were able to crack the nut and bring a new medicine, electricity, or nuclear power. So, it is important that the CEO, the leader, is an optimist who focuses on opportunities. It is also important that he has people around him who provide a diversity of views, including very pessimistic views, because you need to do whatever you do with your eyes wide open. Clearly, it is not my talent to find everything that can go wrong beforehand as much as it is for some of the other people on my team, but it is my talent to see what we can do by combining those two perspectives. I need people who execute underneath to do that. Against that backdrop, what is your advice to young people? My advice to young people is to be yourselves. One of the things you cannot change is who you are. I mean, you can improve at the margins, but you cannot change basic psychological traits—it is very difficult to change them. So, be yourself. Authenticity is number one. If I were to give advice to young people, it would be to never pursue a field that you don't have a great passion for. Having passion is not a condition that will guarantee success; I have seen many passionate people who were not successful. But I have never seen someone who was successful who was not passionate about what they were doing. If you aim for the top, don't choose what your boss is telling you or what your mom is telling you—choose what you love to do. Well, it's very clear that you do what you love. Your passion and optimism come through very clearly. It has been extraordinary to talk to you. Thank you so much. Oh, thank you very, very much.

Silicon Valley’s Case Against The Wealth Tax

Prof G Markets(59.6K)·34:58·30.3K views·2026-05-20
Ed ElsonCo-Host, Prof G Markets Jason CalacanisAngel Investor and Podcast Host Bradley TuskFounder and CEO, Tusk Strategies
TL;DRThe proposed billionaire wealth tax is a politically motivated "asset seizure" that is highly impractical to enforce and risks driving critical tax revenue and jobs out of high-tax states.
TL;DR The proposed billionaire wealth tax is a politically motivated "asset seizure" that is highly impractical to enforce and risks driving critical tax revenue and jobs out of high-tax states. Instead of vilifying the wealthy, policymakers should focus on structural...

TL;DR

The proposed billionaire wealth tax is a politically motivated "asset seizure" that is highly impractical to enforce and risks driving critical tax revenue and jobs out of high-tax states. Instead of vilifying the wealthy, policymakers should focus on structural solutions such as closing margin loan loopholes, deregulating high-cost sectors like healthcare and housing, and enacting voting reforms to empower the moderate majority.

The Core Arguments Against the Proposed Wealth Tax

The proposed 2026 Billionaire Tax Act in California (a one-time 5% tax on assets of $1 billion or more) and its federal counterpart face severe pushback from prominent tech investors and political strategists who argue it is both counterproductive and dangerous.

  • Asset Seizure vs. Traditional Taxation: Investor and podcast host Jason Calacanis argues the wealth tax is not a standard income tax, but a cumbersome asset seizure.
      • It requires highly impractical valuations of illiquid assets, including private company shares, fine art, and personal property.
      • The proposed valuation of founder shares with super-voting rights (valued at voting rights rather than asset value) has already accelerated the exit of major tech founders like Larry Page, Sergey Brin, Mark Zuckerberg, Travis Kalanick, and David Sacks from California.
  • Tax Flight and Revenue Destruction: Bradley Tusk (founder of Tusk Ventures and former Deputy Governor of Illinois) highlights the historical failure of jurisdictional tax increases.
      • Between 2012 and 2022, New York’s share of U.S. millionaires fell from 12.7% to 8.7% due to tax hikes and aggressive regulation.
      • This flight resulted in an estimated $13 billion annual deficit in lost tax revenue that could have funded critical social services like Medicaid and public housing.
  • The "Slippery Slope" Threshold: While the California measure targets billionaires, Tusk warns that the ballot's language allows the state legislature to expand the tax to any group consistent with the referendum's intent. This loophole could easily lower the asset threshold from $1 billion down to $1 million, effectively targeting average California homeowners.

Political Demagoguery and Performative Legislation

The push for wealth taxes is increasingly driven by political opportunism on both sides of the aisle rather than sound fiscal policy.

  • Exploiting Voter Anger: Politicians on the extreme left and right secure votes and donations by stoking class resentment and promising "justice" or "revenge" against successful individuals.
  • The Cost of Performative Politics: Tusk points to New York Assemblyman Zohran Mamdani’s push for a Pied-à-Terre tax, which specifically targeted billionaire Ken Griffin. By making Griffin the face of the fight for political clout, the state risked losing Griffin's planned $6 billion office renovation in Manhattan, which would have created 15,000 jobs for New Yorkers.
  • Safety Concerns: Calacanis warns that the constant political vilification of successful people has created a dangerous environment, resulting in a dramatic rise in credible threats and the need for heavy security details among tech leaders.

Realistic Tax and Labor Alternatives

Rather than implementing unconstitutional wealth taxes, the speakers propose practical, free-market adjustments to address economic inequality.

  • Taxing Margin Loans: A prominent loophole allows individuals with over $10 million in net worth to take out low-interest margin loans against their stock portfolios to fund their lifestyles without triggering capital gains taxes. Calacanis suggests implementing a tax on the origination of these margin loans.
  • Gradual Minimum Wage Increases: Raising the federal minimum wage (currently $7.25) by $1 per year over five years to reach $12 or more could stimulate the economy. Studies from the Nordics, Switzerland, and New Zealand show that higher minimum wages do not significantly impact the cost of consumer goods, but do turn lower-income citizens into active consumers who fuel economic growth.

Structural and Political Reforms to Lower Costs

Addressing inequality requires reducing the cost of living and breaking the political gridlock that prevents moderate, compromise-driven solutions.

  • Deregulating Key Sectors: The three major areas driving middle-class anxiety—housing, education, and healthcare—are heavily monopolized and restricted by government regulations.
      • Deregulating university accreditations, streamlining housing permits (similar to Texas, Nevada, and Florida), and leveraging AI in healthcare could dramatically lower costs. For example, AI-driven diagnostics could reduce the price of a full-body scan from $3,500 to $30.
  • Direct Wealth Transfers: Tusk advocates for Universal Basic Income (UBI) and "Medicare for All" (with a private option) over bureaucratic tax-and-spend programs, noting that up to 40 cents of every tax dollar is lost to government overhead before reaching those in need.
  • Political Voting Reform: To bypass extreme primary voters (who currently dictate the political agenda due to low ~10% primary turnouts), Tusk's organization is advocating for secure mobile voting (`mobilevoting.org`). Increasing primary turnout to 30%–50% would force politicians to cater to the moderate majority and collaborate on practical legislation.

Notable Quotes

"It's a seizure of assets... Tell me everything in your house, Ed. Tell me every painting, tell me every car. Go find somebody to appraise my classic cars... and then we want 5% of those. It is an asset seizure of wealth I’ve already paid taxes on." — Jason Calacanis
"Every time that someone sent us a dollar in tax revenue, by the time that money reached the people in need, 30 to 40 cents on that dollar was gone... If California has less money to help the poor because wealthy people left, then you're not solving the problem of inequality." — Bradley Tusk
"The things that are breaking people are the cost of education, the cost of healthcare, and the cost of homes... We need to dramatically lower the cost of these three things by unleashing the same entrepreneurs who are currently being vilified." — Jason Calacanis
"There are reasonable, achievable solutions to almost every single problem we face... but the only way we can do that is to take the power away from the extremes who currently dictate and control the agenda, and give it back to the middle." — Bradley Tusk
But this is specifically designed, as Bradley astutely pointed out, to vilify the rich, to vilify successful people. And when they hear that you want to put the eye of every socialist lunatic in New York on Ken Griffin's house, or you want to vilify Larry and Sergey, they leave. They don't want to be in a state where they're not wanted. And also, it's dangerous. We've now tipped over with the United Healthcare CEO, Brian Thompson, being murdered. They're literally putting targets on people's backs, and the target they're putting on their back is: "this person is successful, and you should hate them for being successful." Welcome to Prof G Markets, I'm Ed Elson. It is May 20th, let's check in on yesterday's market vitals. The major indices ended the day in the red, with tech stocks declining ahead of Nvidia's earnings due tonight. Brent crude climbed back above $110 per barrel with no progress on Iran, and the yield on 30-year Treasuries hit its highest level since 2007 on long-term energy inflation expectations. Traders are increasingly anticipating a rate hike from the Federal Reserve, and on Kalshi, the odds that we will see a hike this year has climbed to 43%. Okay, what else is happening? Billionaire wealth has more than doubled since 2019, and now voters want to tax it. The 2026 Billionaire Tax Act is officially on the November ballot in California. It would impose a one-time 5% tax on the assets of Californians worth $1 billion or more. The union backing the measure says the goal is to prevent hospital closures across California and fund state programs, but the proposed tax has drawn fierce opposition. Critics argue that wealth taxes target the people driving investment and job creation, and could push them out of the state. And California is just the start. Senator Bernie Sanders and Congressman Ro Khanna have introduced a federal version that would go even further with a 5% annual tax on every American billionaire. So, we figured that this was a good time to have a discussion about the wealth tax with two people who have thought very hard about it. Today we are speaking with Jason Calacanis, investor and host of the All-In Podcast, and also Bradley Tusk, founder and CEO of Tusk Ventures. Jason and Bradley, thank you so much for joining me here on Prof G Markets. Bradley, I'm going to start with you because you've just written about this. I'd like to just sort of set the stage here. We're talking about taxing billionaires not just in California, but also across the rest of the nation at a federal level. How did we get here, and when you look at California, what are the chances that you think this will actually go through? Yeah, there's, in my view, no question that we have an economic equality problem in the United States. The question is how do you best go about solving it? And to me, you know, there's half a dozen ideas that are a lot better than a billionaire's tax. I'm saying this as someone who ran the state of Illinois—I was the deputy governor, I oversaw the state's budget and operations, and so every year I had to figure out how we were going to take in money and then how we were going to distribute it. I know for a fact that every time that someone like Ed Elson sent us a dollar in tax revenue, by the time that money reached the people in need, 30 or 40 cents on that dollar was gone. And so sure, we could make billionaires pay more in taxes, but odds are a lot of that really is just about, you know, further full-time employment for the public sector and public sector unions who are big Democratic donors. And so I do think that people who have more should have to pay more, but I don't think that this is the way to do it. But the reason we're here is there is political opportunity in exploiting the anger of people, and you see that from both sides. Trump is a master of that on the right, and people like Sanders are masters on the left. When you take people's frustrations with their lives and you stoke their anger and you promise them revenge and justice, they will vote for you. And therefore, the more they vote for you, and the more they pay you attention, and the more they donate money, the more you do it. And so we're in this self-fulfilling cycle now where both sides are incentivized to be as demagogic as possible, and ideas like billionaire's taxes or whatever else become very popular. In terms of your second question, Ed, of will this pass, I'm not sure. So, the latest polling that I saw did have it at about 50% support, 28% opposed, and the other 23, whatever it was, undecided. But if you look at the Kalshi prediction market, it's much more like two to one against, so that kind of gives you a mixed thing. On a more basic level, in the 30-something years I've spent in politics, the way I would look at this is to say the average person, when they see the ballot question, is going to say, "Does this affect me? No. Do I think billionaires should pay more? Yes. Do I want to see healthcare cut? No." And so that would argue that people will vote for it. However, there are some pretty fatal flaws, whether just out of sort of greed or stupidity by the proponents. And one of them is that it allows the California legislature to expand this to any group they want, as long as it's consistent with the intent and purposes of the referendum. The Digital Fairness Act: Keynote Address by Isabelle Pérignon

IIEA(17.9K)·24:45·3 views·2026-05-20
Isabelle PérignonDirector for Consumer Policy, European Commission
TL;DRThe upcoming Digital Fairness Act (DFA)—a flagship initiative of EU Commissioner Michael McGrath scheduled for adoption under the Irish EU Presidency—aims to modernize consumer protection by banning manipulative online practices like dark patterns and dynamic pricing.
TL;DR The upcoming Digital Fairness Act (DFA)—a flagship initiative of EU Commissioner Michael McGrath scheduled for adoption under the Irish EU Presidency—aims to modernize consumer protection by banning manipulative online practices like dark patterns and dynamic pricing. To...

TL;DR

The upcoming Digital Fairness Act (DFA)—a flagship initiative of EU Commissioner Michael McGrath scheduled for adoption under the Irish EU Presidency—aims to modernize consumer protection by banning manipulative online practices like dark patterns and dynamic pricing. To ensure these rules are effectively enforced, the European Commission will simultaneously introduce a major reform of the Consumer Protection Cooperation (CPC) Regulation to drastically accelerate investigations and enable direct penalties for cross-border violations.

The Strategic Objectives of the Digital Fairness Act

  • The 2030 Consumer Agenda: This overarching policy framework operates on the principle that consumer protection and economic competitiveness are mutually reinforcing.
      • Economic Impact: Consumer purchases represent approximately 51% of the European Union's GDP, making consumer choice a primary driver of the EU economy.
      • Market Integrity: A healthy market must rely on consumers making choices based on price, quality, and product characteristics rather than manipulative digital tactics.
  • Two Core Priorities:
      • Vulnerable Consumers: Special protections are being designed for minors, individuals with disabilities, and those who lack digital literacy.
      • Regulatory Simplification: The Commission aims to simplify rules and reduce unnecessary administrative burdens for businesses by closing legal gaps rather than adding redundant regulatory layers.

Tackling "Generation 2.0" Unfair Digital Practices

  • Inadequacy of Legacy Laws: Traditional frameworks, such as the 20-year-old Unfair Commercial Practices Directive, are struggling to address highly sophisticated, psychologically manipulative online architectures.
  • Key Digital Targets of the DFA:
      • Dark Patterns: Interface designs that subtly steer consumer behavior beneath conscious awareness (e.g., making cookie acceptance a single click while hiding refusal options behind complex menus). The DFA aims to establish a list of specific prohibited dark patterns across the EU.
      • Dynamic Pricing: Discriminatory practices where prices spike unexpectedly while consumers are waiting in virtual queues (e.g., ticket prices multiplying several-fold at checkout).
      • Subscription Traps: Intentionally complex processes for canceling online contracts, and "free trial" models that automatically renew into costly, long-term subscriptions.
      • Minors and Addictive Design: Features targeting youth, such as video game "loot boxes" and addictive user-experience designs that encourage excessive screen time and spending.

Fostering a Level Playing Field for Businesses

  • Preventing Single Market Fragmentation: The European Commission wants to avoid a regulatory patchwork where EU member states enact differing national laws (such as recently observed national variations in influencer marketing laws).
      • An EU-level harmonized framework will provide much-needed legal certainty for businesses operating across all 27 member states.
  • Promoting Quality Over Manipulation: By cracking down on manipulative designs, the DFA seeks to reward compliant, high-quality businesses that compete transparently rather than those using deceptive tactics to capture market share.
  • Regulatory Synergy: The DFA is designed to act as a horizontal safety net, complementing existing major digital regulations like the Digital Services Act (DSA), the Digital Markets Act (DMA), and the AI Act without creating conflicting compliance burdens.

Reforming Cross-Border Consumer Law Enforcement

  • Current Bottlenecks: Enforcement of EU consumer law currently takes an average of 18 months, and national authorities are frequently limited to securing voluntary commitments from non-compliant platforms.
  • CPC Regulation Reform: Alongside the DFA, the Commission will introduce a revised Consumer Protection Cooperation (CPC) Regulation to strengthen the network of 30 national enforcement authorities (the 27 EU member states plus EEA partners).
  • Enhanced Enforcement Powers:
      • Stronger Deterrents: Introducing faster investigative procedures and the power to impose substantial fines for cross-border violations.
      • Platform Suspension: Granting authorities the power to suspend access to online platforms if they pose an imminent threat to consumers.

Notable Quotes

"Competitiveness and consumer policy are not in tension, but they are really mutually reinforcing. Consumers are the largest economic actors in the single market, with their purchases accounting for around 51% of the EU GDP." — Isabelle Pérignon, Director for Consumer Policy, European Commission
