They know their habits, they know their preferences, and even their purchasing power, while users are often left to interact only with interfaces, call centers, or chatbots... Asymmetries and unfairness in business-to-consumer commercial practices are therefore deeper than ever." — Isabelle Pérignon, Director for Consumer Policy, European Commission

This podcast is brought to you by the IIEA. Sharing ideas, shaping policy. Good afternoon everybody, and welcome to today's webinar on the Digital Fairness Act. My name is Joyce O'Connor, and I chair the Digital Group here at the IIEA. It is my great pleasure to welcome you all to this timely discussion on one of the most important and indeed fast-evolving areas of digital policy in Europe. Our keynote speaker is Isabelle Pérignon, Director for Consumer Affairs in the European Commission. Isabelle, we're so pleased to have you make the presentation today. I know you have a very, very busy day, so we really appreciate you being with us, and thank you very much for that. Isabelle will speak to us for about 20 minutes or so, and then I will go to you, our audience, for Q&A using the Q&A function at the bottom of the screen. Please feel free to send in your comments or questions during the presentation, and I'll come back to them once Isabelle has finished her presentation. When you send in a question, I'd very much appreciate if you'd give your name and affiliation. Thank you very much. Today's presentation and Q&A are on the record. Join us also on X using the handle @IIEA. As digital technologies increasingly shape every aspect of our lives, from how we shop and communicate to how information is presented and decisions are influenced online, questions of fairness, transparency, consumer protection, and accountability have moved to the center of public policy and regulatory debate. The Digital Fairness Act is a flagship initiative of Commissioner Michael McGrath that the Commission will adopt at the end of the year under the Irish presidency. The Digital Fairness Act will modernize EU consumer law by addressing manipulative digital practices that undermine autonomy and choice. It will also ensure that the digital single market operates fairly, fostering trust and enabling businesses to compete on quality rather than on manipulative tactics. Compliance and enforcement will also be facilitated. The Consumer Protection Regulation will also be revised and introduce faster investigative procedures and stronger deterrents, including fines for cross-border violations. This consumer protection framework will be responding to the key challenges of the digital age. It will also reflect the broader EU approach to digital regulation and how Europe can balance innovation and competitiveness with the protection of fundamental rights and consumer interests in the digital age. I'm really pleased to welcome you again, Isabelle Pérignon. From her bio, you will see that Isabelle has played an influential role in shaping European approaches to digital governance and has the experience of working at the intersection of innovation, regulation, consumer rights, and the digital economy. Her insights are especially valuable at a time when policymakers, regulators, industry leaders, and civil society are addressing these challenges, and how best to build trust, protect consumers, and foster innovation in an increasingly complex digital environment. I now have the great pleasure of inviting Isabelle to make her presentation. Over to you, Isabelle, and thank you. Thank you very much, Mrs. Chair, and good afternoon to all of you. Thank you very much again for your invitation to present indeed this flagship, which is the flagship of Commissioner McGrath and that we intend to adopt at the end of this year: the famous Digital Fairness Act. Since November 2025, the European Commission has had a new comprehensive blueprint for consumer policy, and that is the 2030 Consumer Agenda. At its heart lie two closely connected principles. First, competitiveness and consumer policy are not in tension, but they are really mutually reinforcing. Consumers are the largest economic actors in the single market, with their purchases accounting for around 51% of the EU GDP. Their preferences and choices actively shape the direction of our economy in Europe. However, in a healthy and fair competitive market, consumers should benefit from a wide range of products and services offered at the best possible conditions. Their choices should be guided by factors such as price, quality, and product characteristics, and should not be influenced by manipulative or distortive market practices. Second, consumer policy plays a crucial stabilizing role, particularly in times of uncertainty. Citizens feel more confident when they know that businesses operate within a clear and reliable framework of rules and that effective remedies are available when their rights are not respected. Consumer protection is really close to people's everyday lives, to you as consumers. It is an area where everyone has direct experience and can clearly perceive its concrete value. So, it is really a powerful instrument for promoting fairness in all commercial practices, whether online or offline. The 2030 Consumer Agenda is structured around a set of key priorities that reflect the evolving challenges of the single market. It focuses in particular on strengthening enforcement, enhancing online consumer protection and digital fairness, promoting sustainable consumption, addressing remaining barriers for consumers across the single market, and improving governance through closer cooperation with all stakeholders. So, across all these areas, there are two overarching priorities guiding this approach: special attention to consumers in situations of vulnerability—people who do not know exactly how to use the internet, people who have disabilities, minors—and also the need to simplify rules while reducing unnecessary burdens for businesses. We want simplification; we want to reduce the amount of overlapping legislation. Really, together, these measures aim to ensure that the benefits of the single market in Europe are accessible to all, while maintaining a fair and inclusive environment. This is also our objective when it comes to the digital single market. Indeed, the technological revolution has profoundly transformed every aspect of our daily lives. Two decades ago, it was not really easy to order items from the other side of the world, or to request a cab while knowing exactly when it would arrive and how much it would cost. Today, every product is within closer reach and every service is faster. However, these benefits do not come for free. They carry the significant cost of granting access to our data, enabling the rise of hyper-personalized, targeted commercial practices for which we, as consumers, have been largely unprepared, given the complexities of e-commerce and digital marketing. Today, traders and sellers can know almost everything about consumers. They know their habits, they know their preferences, and even their purchasing power, while users are often left to interact only with interfaces, call centers, or chatbots when they need to engage with someone online. So, asymmetries and unfairness in business-to-consumer commercial practices are therefore deeper than ever. It is thus up to the regulators, meaning us, to reduce this gap and to help consumers navigate the opportunities of e-commerce in a safe and well-informed manner. Traditional consumer protection law contains principles that remain valid. However, even the most effective instruments, such as the Unfair Commercial Practices Directive, which we have had at the EU level for more than 20 years, and the Consumer Rights Directive, which has existed for so many years in Europe, are struggling to address these new practices. Even if we have principle-based rules, they are not always fully fit to address emerging unfair practices. Indeed, specific unfair practices have emerged that are designed to push or strongly steer consumers, including what we call dark patterns, addictive design features, unfair personalization, and influencer marketing. This can be seen as a sort of 2.0 generation of unfair commercial practices. They operate through subtle, often invisible mechanisms that shape the behavior of consumers beneath the level of conscious awareness, making it particularly difficult for us to recognize or to resist these dark patterns. For instance, dark patterns guide the consumer through a path that appears to be intuitive, but is in fact carefully designed to steer our decisions. Certain options are highlighted, easier to select, or preselected by default, while others are hidden, requiring more steps, or we need to be really, you know, very clear about what this confusing way could be. For example, when we accept tracking cookies online, that might take just one click. Sometimes refusing these additional cookies takes much more time. As a result, the consumer may end up accepting conditions, making purchases, or sharing data in a way that does not entirely reflect their genuine preferences. Therefore, among all the possible options and solutions that we are carefully analyzing now in the European Commission with my services, it is very important that, in the context of the impact assessment we are preparing on the Digital Fairness Act, we see a list of very specific measures. For example, measures on dark patterns could be prohibited across the European Union in all circumstances, following the approach that we already have with existing legislation at the EU level, such as the Unfair Commercial Practices Directive. This example, I think, captures well the kind of situation that consumers now routinely face online. So, this is important to assess in the same way that we want what is being done offline to be done online. The Digital Fairness Act, which we are aiming to present by the end of this year, seeks precisely to ensure that EU consumer law is fit to respond to these new challenges. Of course, we want to build on extensive studies that we carried out, such as the fitness check report that we adopted in October 2024. We focus, as I said, on dark patterns, addictive design, unfair personalization targeting consumers' vulnerabilities, and untransparent marketing by social media influencers. It also seeks, through this Digital Fairness Act, to tackle other commonly reported problems that consumers face online. For example, with dynamic pricing. I am sure that you have all experienced the difficulty of trying to get a ticket for your favorite sports event or your favorite concert. You are in this endless queue, and then when finally you want to buy the ticket, which was advertised at a price of 100—100 euros, for example, suddenly you find out that the price is three times or even four times higher. So, this very important and very discriminatory practice, dynamic pricing, is really a big concern that we want to address in the Digital Fairness Act. We also want to address difficulties with the cancellation of contracts concluded online. Again, we have all experienced the fact that we want to conclude a contract online, which is very easy to do, but it is very difficult to cancel. We also want to find a way to address automatic renewals of subscriptions. You start with a free trial, and then suddenly you find out that it is no longer free, and you are stuck in a long-term contract. We really want to have this tackled in the Digital Fairness Act. So, the Digital Fairness Act is meant to be a pro-consumer tool, but it is also very much a pro-business initiative. Indeed, we really want to ensure that businesses compete fairly on a level playing field—on innovation, quality, and customer value—and not in a fragmented market at the level of the Union. Indeed, what we think is that it is important to offer companies and our businesses in Europe a healthy and fair market. Businesses should be able to rely on transparency, allowing well-informed consumers to choose products and services based on quality rather than on manipulative digital tactics that in fact attract consumers in a very misleading manner. In this sense, we try to reduce such practices that may benefit smaller players and more compliant businesses. We want to help the businesses that comply with the rules in Europe to be able to compete on very equal terms, in a very level playing field, with other businesses that apply the rules we have in Europe. What is key, for example, is that we avoid fragmentation at the EU level. We have, for example, the case recently of different influencer legislation. At the national level, different member states now want to have different legislation addressing the issue of influencers who tell you what to buy and how to buy. When you see that there is a patchwork of different national systems, we think it is important to regulate at the EU level so that we can offer a harmonized framework for companies. In this sense, we consider that the Digital Fairness Act needs to increase legal certainty for businesses, offering them clear rules on unfair digital commercial practices, but also offering them clear rules to facilitate compliance and to facilitate enforcement by national enforcing authorities. We want to avoid fragmentation; we want to avoid the fact that a company has to deal with 27 different regulatory frameworks. We want to help and push for more competitiveness in Europe. This simplification element is a dimension that we take very seriously. Commissioner McGrath held implementation dialogues last year, in July, where we invited businesses and companies, and we were seeking together solutions to avoid this fragmentation and to have clearer rules for businesses. In that perspective, again, we do not want to come with additional rules. On the contrary, we want to fill the gaps that we have identified. We know that we already have several rules applying in Europe. You have the Digital Services Act, the famous DSA; we have the Digital Markets Act, the famous DMA; we have also the AI Act and all the consumer legislation that I was mentioning. The objective is not to add another layer, but rather to see the complementarity, to clarify, and to simplify what we can simplify through this Digital Fairness Act. So, we want, through this instrument, to strengthen this horizontal safety net that will apply to all traders, irrespective of the technology or the business model used. Some of the measures we are considering are building on our existing consumer protection rules. For example, we can make it clearer for companies which new kinds of dark patterns the authorities want to tackle, because we see new phenomena. There is always a lot of creativity around dark patterns, and we need to explain clearly to businesses what is allowed, what is possible, and what is prohibited. That is why, in this Digital Fairness Act, we want to have clarity for business, and we also want to pay particular attention to the protection of minors. Minors, as we have heard and seen, are a key concern now in many member states and also here in Brussels, at the European Commission. Our President, Ursula von der Leyen, convened a special panel of experts to really address this issue of the safety of children online, addressing everything that we were carefully proposing under the Digital Fairness Act. We want to address the issue of the protection of minors through, for example, certain features like the famous loot boxes that you can have in certain video games. We also want, we also want to address some features specifically for children as concerns addictive designs and special personalized practices. Along with this Digital Fairness Act, you must have understood that what is key is enforcement. It is nice to have rules, but it is very important to be able to enforce them. Enforcement in Europe is done at a national level, but we want to help these national authorities by having a strengthened system of coordination for enforcement actions. So far, we have different instruments in Europe to fight for and to enforce our consumer rules, but the most important one is what we call the Consumer Protection Cooperation, the CPC network. It is a network composed of all the different national authorities, the 27 national authorities, and we also have countries outside Europe from the European Economic Area that participate in it, so we are a network of 30 national enforcement authorities. The objective is to also present at the end of the year, together as a package with the Digital Fairness Act, reinforcing rules on the CPC Regulation. Why? Because we see now that it is very difficult to enforce consumer rules. We see that on average it takes more than 18 months, which is huge and very long, to enforce our consumer legislation. Most of the time, the legislation that we have today only enables these national authorities to come away with voluntary commitments from the companies, and obviously, it is not enough. We need to be tougher, we need to have more and very much more efficient powers to impose fines, and we need to have a really more deterrent effect. We need to have powers at the European level to impose fines, and we need to have powers to suspend access to a platform if need be, if we see that there are imminent threats on these platforms. This reform of the CPC is very important, and it is also a very important flagship of Commissioner McGrath. It will come together as a package with the Digital Fairness Act, and both of them are expected for adoption at the end of this year. So, you will see that the Irish presidency is key for us. It will be a key presidency for consumer policy, and I will be very happy, I hope, to meet you in person next time when I will be traveling to your beautiful country. Thank you again for your attention, and I am happy to take any questions you may wish. This podcast is brought to you by the IIEA. Sharing ideas, shaping policy.

The future of U.S. foreign policy: Competing with China, U.S. reliability and a new warfare era

MS NOW(9.8M)·30:57·9.6K views·2026-05-20
Antony BlinkenFormer Secretary of State Jen PsakiHost, MS NOW Linda Thomas-GreenfieldUS Ambassador to the United Nations
TL;DRTo successfully compete with systemic rivals like China and navigate a new era of high-tech warfare, the United States must abandon unilateralism, rebuild its damaged international credibility, and foster agile, issue-specific global alliances.
TL;DR To successfully compete with systemic rivals like China and navigate a new era of high-tech warfare, the United States must abandon unilateralism, rebuild its damaged international credibility, and foster agile, issue-specific global alliances. The Unilateral Deficit:...

TL;DR

To successfully compete with systemic rivals like China and navigate a new era of high-tech warfare, the United States must abandon unilateralism, rebuild its damaged international credibility, and foster agile, issue-specific global alliances.

China and the Power of Multilateral Alliances

  • The Unilateral Deficit: Former Secretary of State Antony Blinken warned that competing with China one-on-one is a losing proposition due to China's massive market, superior purchasing power parity, and manufacturing output, which is currently three times that of the U.S.
  • The Power of Convergence: Building allied coalitions is the primary strategic counterweight to China.
      • Aligning the U.S. with Europe, Japan, South Korea, India, Australia, and Canada shifts the coalition's economic weight from 20–25% of global GDP to 50–60%, making U.S.-led initiatives impossible for China to ignore.
  • Declining Global Trust: A poll of 130 countries revealed that China is currently viewed as a more credible international actor than the U.S. In Germany, a poll showed that 70% of respondents viewed the U.S. as an adversary.
  • African Alignment: Ambassador Linda Thomas-Greenfield noted that African nations are increasingly forced to choose sides, frequently favoring China because they currently view it as a more trustworthy and reliable partner than the U.S.

Rebuilding U.S. Reliability and Diplomatic Credibility

  • The Reliability Question: Senator Andy Kim emphasized that the primary question foreign leaders ask about the U.S. is: *"Is America a reliable country?"* Rebuilding this baseline of consistency is a long-term challenge that will outlast any single presidential administration.
  • Diplomatic Understaffing: The U.S. is severely hamstringing its own foreign policy by failing to confirm diplomatic personnel.
      • The U.S. currently lacks Senate-confirmed ambassadors in over 100 countries, including 37 nations in Africa. This vacancy rate cripples the nation's ability to negotiate complex agreements or counter foreign influence.
  • The Cost of Domestic Corruption: Ambassador Thomas-Greenfield pointed out that reports of a past U.S. administration financially benefiting to the tune of $1.4 billion severely undercut American moral authority. This makes human rights and anti-corruption advocacy abroad appear hypocritical to foreign governments.

Strategic Failures and Regional Fallout in the Middle East

  • The Strategic Deficit in Iran: Blinken characterized the U.S. conflict with Iran as a strategic failure. Despite any tactical successes, Iran is poised to retain its regime, its missile capabilities, vestiges of its nuclear program, and significant leverage over the Strait of Hormuz shipping lanes.
  • Economic and Diplomatic Strain:
      • Domestic Costs: Senator Kim highlighted that the conflict has cost American consumers over $42 billion in increased gas and diesel prices.
      • Alienated Allies: U.S. actions have alienated European allies (previously strained by disputes over NATO, Greenland, and Denmark) and frustrated Gulf partners who feel directly exposed to Iranian retaliation. Notably, Iran has fired more projectiles at the UAE than at Israel during this conflict.
  • Diplomatic Off-Ramps: Resolving the standoff will require highly skilled negotiation and compromise. Potential terms include down-blending Iran's highly enriched uranium, a temporary enrichment moratorium, and a Strait of Hormuz tolling system to fund regional reconstruction.

The War in Ukraine and the Future of Warfare

  • Resilience on the Battlefield: Despite declining U.S. funding, Ukraine is holding its military and economic lines with European aid. Meanwhile, Russia’s war economy is beginning to cannibalize itself as defense spending fails to generate productive domestic infrastructure.
  • Weapons of Mass Production: Senator Kim warned that modern warfare has shifted from "weapons of mass destruction" to "weapons of mass production." The battlefield is now defined by the rapid, high-volume manufacturing of drones, AI tools (such as Mithos), and ballistic missiles.
  • Rapid Tech Acceleration: The pace of global change is unprecedented. Blinken noted that while the telephone took 75 years to reach 50 million users, Instagram took 4 years, and ChatGPT reached the milestone in just 4 months.
  • Variable Geometry: Future global governance will move away from rigid autocracy-versus-democracy blocs. Instead, the U.S. must lead via "variable geometry"—agile, issue-specific coalitions of countries, companies, and civil actors collaborating on targeted problems like climate change, AI governance, and food security.

Notable Quotes

"If we're competing with China one-on-one, that's a game that we may lose... But when we're aligned with Europe, with Japan, with Korea, with India, with Australia, with Canada, we go from about 20, 25% of world GDP to 50 or 60%, a lot harder for China to ignore." — *Antony Blinken, Former Secretary of State*
"It makes us look like hypocrites. It is a very hard conversation to have with a foreign government where you know you're talking to them about corruption in their country and they're going to talk back to you about what's happening in your country." — *Linda Thomas-Greenfield, Ambassador*
"We are finding ourselves in a place where we are trying to navigate these weapons of mass production—this idea of what it is when you're able to produce such quantity, with drones, ballistic missiles... That is the future." — *Sen. Andy Kim*
"There's a lot of understandable nostalgia for the past... But the reality is you can't put the genie back in the bottle. You've got to shape a new bottle, and that's what these conversations have to all be about." — *Antony Blinken, Former Secretary of State*
Hi everyone. First of all, we all recognize it is difficult to follow Governor Gavin Newsom in a conversation about slush funds that included the reference to kneepads. So, we are going to do our best because these are very important topics, too. And obviously, there is so much going on in the world right now. We're going to try to cover a lot of it, but the whole purpose of this forum is to talk about ideas and what to do in the future and how we move forward. So we're going to have a conversation about that, too. But I want to just start with China because I think this is a topic that is on a lot of people's minds. It is always on a lot of people's minds, but certainly after the President just got back from a trip to China last week. I want to just start with you, Secretary Blinken. You said—and you've done a lot of speeches, we looked at a lot of your speeches—you said if the United States is competing with China alone, we may well wind up on the losing side of the equation. Where do you think the relationship stands now? What is your assessment of that? And what is your prescription on where it really needs to go from here? Well, thanks very much, Jen. It's great to be with everyone, with my friends and colleagues as well. Look, I think we have a President who divides the world up into what he sees as spheres of influence, and the big countries get to do what they want in their part of the world and the smaller countries have to suffer what they will, and that includes China. And so other than some trade issues that may affect us, his inclination is to give them free rein over their region and maybe even a little bit beyond. And I think we saw that play out. The challenge is what happens within one sphere of influence almost never stays there. And countries are always looking for more, and China's an example of that, as well. This is the one country that has the capacity, as well as the intent, to reshape the international system in a way that advances its interests and values and probably undermines ours. I think, Jen, as we're looking at it, we have to resist trying to put it on a bumper sticker. There's no neat way of summing up what is probably the most complex and consequential relationship. We compete, for sure. We contest where we really disagree, and we have to find ways to cooperate on issues that really are in the interest of both of our peoples. But from that perspective, you've got to approach it from a position of strength, and that means making ourselves as strong as possible at home, with the right investments. But it also means making ourselves as strong as possible around the world, aligning other countries, building convergence, because, to the point you made, if we're competing with China one-on-one, that's a game that we may lose. Their market's much bigger, their manufacturing is three times our manufacturing, purchasing power parity is greater than ours, more papers, more patents, a bigger navy—you can go down the list. But when we're aligned with Europe, with Japan, with Korea, with India, with Australia, with Canada, we go from about 20 or 25% of world GDP to 50 or 60%. That is a lot harder for China to ignore. Unfortunately, where we are now is, just as the President seems to be underwater on a lot of issues within the United States, we're underwater around the world. There was a poll out a couple of weeks ago of 130 countries, and what it found was that China was seen as a more credible actor than the United States. In Germany, 70% of people in one poll saw the United States as an adversary. So, this means that our ability to align, to build convergence in ways that can deal with the challenge that China poses has been vastly diminished, and that's a real challenge going forward. And that's sort of a perfect setup for you, Ambassador. I mean, everybody knows who the Ambassador is, but she spent not just time living up in the apartment in New York engaging with other UN ambassadors, but also decades serving around the world in the Foreign Service. And one of the things about these trips is these countries, to your point, countries around the world are evaluating not just the United States but also the strength of the leader that you're meeting with. I wonder if you're thinking about how President Xi and other potential partners, and some current allies that Tony referenced, look at this trip. How do they look at this trip, and how do you kind of repair these relationships and try to rebuild that type of coalition that he was referencing? Secretary Blinken—I'm sorry, I worked with him before, I'll keep calling him the Secretary—go ahead. I think—am I on? I think the entire world was watching Trump's trip to China, and they were watching holding their breaths because they knew that whatever came out of this trip would not be good for them. And so they were waiting to see. I mean, imagine the people of Taiwan and what they are thinking, and all of our friends in Russia, in Europe, what they were thinking. And I think even more than anybody, the Russians were watching this very closely, because when we were in New York, Russia and China were friends without limits. They were the bros; they kept very close connections with each other. Now China has played a very interesting game, and Trump, where now we are talking to the Chinese about things that the Russians might feel uncomfortable about. So, we're seeing the pieces of the chessboard moving around in ways that has all the pawns feeling uncomfortable. You know, what is Africa? We're telling Africa, "You need to make some choices about what you want to see in your future." And they're being forced into making choices related to China because, again, I think they find China much more trustworthy as a partner than the United States. How this will boil down, or what it will boil down to in the UN, remains to be seen, but I think China's prowess, its power, its leadership on the multilateral stage is increasing. Senator, part of our objective today is to certainly talk about the path forward. And, you know, in terms of building strength here at home and building these relationships, it requires tough choices at home at times. There has been bipartisan support, for example, of something that the Ambassador referenced, which is supporting Taiwan. We don't know where that stands exactly right now. What are the tough choices you think Congress and other leaders need to make over the next couple of years in order to position this relationship in a more constructive way? Well, one thing that I think was absolutely spot-on is talking about strength, talking about credibility that the Ambassador and the Secretary talked about. But I want to throw one more word into the mix here that I hear a lot from foreign leaders. I had this one leader of an Asian country call me and talk about just the challenges that they see when it comes to US-China, and just writ large with foreign policy. And they said to me, they just said, "I want you to know that when we talk about America, we ask this question: Is America a reliable country?" We know the answer to what that is. And so I want to just really throw out that sense of reliability, and how important that is, and how much we are struggling with it and why we will continue to struggle as a country even after Trump is gone. This is something that we're going to have to rebuild. So when we're thinking through in Congress how we are making these trade-offs, we also have to be thinking about the importance of credibility, of reliability in terms of what we're actually able to put forward, even when we're talking about some of our strategic advantages, for instance. All of us agree that we need to be thinking closely about how our chip manufacturing and other types of AI tools are so critical to the economy of the future. But how are we able to tell other countries what we think they should be doing when it comes to chips or manufacturing equipment if we don't even know what the White House is going to do when it comes to chips to China and elsewhere? You know, when we're talking about all these different tools at our disposal, the problem is really whether or not we will be consistent with that. And seeing over the course of my time in Congress how Republicans had one tune on China for the past few years, and then all of a sudden are absolutely silent right now—not a single one talking to me about anything that they were concerned about over the past few years—it really just shows how much Trump has captured the Republican Party on China. We're no longer able to talk about this with the strategy that is necessary for what is the most defining competition of our generation. Another area where there seems to have been some capturing, shall we say, is on the war with Iran, where there still has not been a war powers vote that has passed. Come close, you peeled off some people. But let me ask you, Secretary Blinken, right now, I think everybody would like to see this war come to a close. You can certainly tell us how that's going to happen if you know. But what do you see the impact of what we're looking at right now, both, not just with Iran, but in the region and just kind of the US's role in the world 3 years, 5 years, 10 years from now? Well, I think, Jen, we have an example here with Iran of a war choice that never should have happened, and a war that really sends almost a split screen. You can have tactical success in individual areas, and yet have a real strategic failure. And that's where we are. Because at the end of the day, when the dust settles, it's likely that Iran will retain some vestiges of its nuclear program. It will certainly retain a lot of missiles; apparently, they haven't in fact been destroyed. It will retain its regime, and it will have gained this incredible leverage that it didn't have before in the Strait of Hormuz where it will, one way or another, more or less control what happens there because it will have retained the ability to mess with shipping that's going through. And that means even in a small way, if one guy with a shoulder-fired missile can damage a ship, all of a sudden the ships get scared, the insurers get scared, and you've got a real problem, which means they're going to be in on the deal going forward. That radically changes the region, and it's going to take time to work out from that. You're going to have to find ways over time to work around the hold that Iran will have on the Strait by building more pipelines. It is not the best thing, but it's probably the necessary thing to do, especially by focusing on renewables and other ways so that people are not under the thumb of the Iranians, and it really doesn't take much. But it's reshaped it in other ways, too. We've alienated pretty much everyone in doing this. We've alienated the Europeans because we were kicking dirt in their face for 2 years over NATO, over Greenland, over Denmark, you name it. We didn't tell them about this, and then, when the going got tough, we said, "Hey, we need you," and all of a sudden they weren't so eager to do it. Our partners in Asia, they're the ones who have been on the receiving end of the real brunt of this in terms of the immediate impacts of prices and availability. And in a different way, our partners in the Gulf, who to Andy's point, have seen how unreliable we are. Unlike the Europeans, unlike our Asia-Pacific partners, some of them would like the United States to quote-unquote "finish the job." That's not happening, so they're frustrated. Others feel like they've now been subjected to Iran's wrath. Iran has fired more missiles and projectiles at the UAE than it's fired at Israel in this conflict. It's radically changed the equation. I think there is an off-ramp, but the President's going to have to decide whether he wants to take it. And the off-ramp involves, guess what, diplomacy and a compromise. The only two times the Iranians have made a deal in the past were to end the war with Iraq that went on for 8 years with 200,000 Iranian dead—the UN came in, offered them a way forward, got the Iraqi troops out of Iran. The only other time, the JCPOA, the Iran nuclear deal that President Obama negotiated, it took 2 and a half years to get it, they were given some things, we got a lot, and we were in a much better place as a result. I think right now, if the President's looking at this, you're going to have to have some kind of arrangement that maybe down-blends some of their highly enriched uranium, probably has a moratorium on enrichment for some period of time, which would be a good thing. And on the Strait of Hormuz, there'll probably be some kind of tolling system, which no one will want to call by its name, where the proceeds are used for reconstruction of countries in the area that have been damaged. That's a lot to swallow because so much damage was done for so little gain. Is there a scenario, Ambassador, where this war ends and the Iranians don't have some control over the Strait of Hormuz, some financial control? Do you think that's possible? Honestly, I don't. It will be difficult to get to. And part of the reason it will be difficult is, again, we're not negotiating with them. Why are we not negotiating with them? We don't have people to do it. We don't have confirmed, Senate-confirmed ambassadors in over 100 countries. So who is going to negotiate with the Iranians? And I've heard over and over again—Tony, you've dealt with them—that they are extraordinary negotiators. We had extraordinary negotiators; we don't anymore. In Africa, where maybe we want to get some support, 37 countries do not have ambassadors. So the administration is taking the power that it has and dumping it, and so it's in a position where it is not able to negotiate an agreement that would quickly end this war. I think the only thing that will do it is the President will declare victory, which means he has accepted defeat, and move out of the area saying we've accomplished everything we've accomplished, when we know that he has not. Senator Kim, when you just follow some of the headlines, you see kind of war powers vote, war powers vote, war powers vote failing week after week. But there have been some individuals peeled off, I should say, who have voted with Democrats for a War Powers Act, which hasn't happened, I should say, in quite a long time. Give us a sense of where you see things right now in that regard. Are there other ways that you think there is a bipartisan opportunity to hold people in this administration to account over this war? Well, first of all, I want to say that even the war powers approach, this is the wrong approach that we should have been going down. This is not a situation where we should normalize the idea that a president can start a war on their own and then it's up to Congress later to disapprove of it, right? So just from the outset, this is wrong. This is an illegal, unconstitutional war. Now, we are unfortunately in a situation where we do not have three functioning branches of government right now. We have one branch of government that is simply trying to dominate the other two branches of government. I often call Speaker Johnson "Secretary Johnson" because he acts more like a cabinet secretary than he does as a leader of a separate branch of government. So, that's the world that we're in right now. So yes, we're trying to operate with the tools that we can to shine light and draw attention to the American people, who by the way fully see this for what it is. You know, when I'm back home in Jersey, I mean across the political spectrum, nobody likes this war. And they understand just the costs that they are bearing—over $42 billion more that Americans have spent on gas and diesel since the beginning of this war. That's what we are trying to draw attention to, that drumbeat. So we have to make sure that we're not getting caught up on just the process between the different branches of government. This is ultimately about the people, and this is ultimately about the impacts that it is having on them, and that this is a choice that this President made, not just to start a war or not, but whether to help American people or not. And he has clearly made a decision to enrich himself and his family and his friends at the expense of the American people, and they see that so clearly when it comes to Iran. I'm so glad you took the question in that direction. It was spicy and good. Ambassador, you have spent so much time working in so many parts of the world where there are high levels of corruption, where human rights is a key talking point when you sit down with these leaders. When people, let's say in the future, when future ambassadors, future secretaries are going into meetings and they are looking at a past administration that financially benefited to the tune of $1.4 billion—not my numbers, numbers that have been widely reported—and more, what impact does that have on the conversations? It makes us look like hypocrites. It is a very hard conversation to have with a foreign government where you know you're talking to them about corruption in their country and they're going to talk back to you about what's happening in your country. I think the only thing that will get us through this, when there's a change of administration, is that we hold those people accountable. If we hold them accountable, then we can go to other countries and say, "Yes, we had this problem, but accountability is important and this is what we're doing to hold them accountable." Now, will that happen? I don't know, but I would have a hard time having that conversation now with any country because I would feel like a hypocrite, even though I feel strongly that corruption in government is bad, but to try to tell a country that from this side of the Atlantic would be hard. I want to turn—this is going to seem like a sharp turn, but I don't feel like there's nearly enough attention always on the war in Ukraine because of the war with Iran, because of corruption, because of all the other issues. And Secretary Blinken spent so much time as Secretary of State helping build coalitions, as did the Ambassador, of course. If you were to strip away the headlines about Russia, about Ukraine, about military battles, what should people in this room or people watching really understand about the state of things, we're now 4 years into this war? So, Jen, I think two things. First, it's always good to remember why we got into this in the first place, why we were so determined in our support for Ukraine, because of course it was an aggression against a country, and one big country aggressing a smaller country. No one likes to see that, and that tugs on the heartstrings. But there was something else going on. It was an aggression against the very principles at the heart of an international system, no matter how challenged, that really mattered and mattered in working to keep the peace, working to help countries advance and develop, and principles like sovereignty, like independence, like territorial integrity. That was the goal of the Russian aggression as much as it was to erase Ukraine and subsume it into Russia, and that's why we were so invested in it. But right now, what we've seen is something quite remarkable, which is a country of amazing resilience, a country that now, even with the United States receding in the role that it was playing, is still holding its own. Now, Europe has stepped up in ways that are really, really important, including with funding that is so necessary, but you see the Ukrainians on the battlefield holding their own at horrific cost—horrific cost to them, to their civilians and citizens, also at horrific cost to the people that Putin continues to throw into this meat grinder of his own making. But they are holding on, and more than holding on, they're actually taking the fight to Russia. So, I don't think that line is likely to move very much. They're holding their own economically, and they're building a new economy, again, with help from Europe and from others. And the interesting thing is, with their defense industry and what they're doing with drones, they may well wind up at the heart of Europe's future defense industry. They have technology and they have techniques that we don't have, and we see that being spread around the world. And then, step-by-step, they continue to build their democracy, getting closer to Europe, strengthening the tools. You've seen many people who are in high positions now being challenged on what we were just talking about, corruption. So, I think right now, my hope is that the pressure on Putin is such, it's finally catching up with him. He had a reprieve with Iran and being able to sell some oil in the higher prices, but everything else across the board in Russia is really turning. And he's built a war economy that worked for a while, but now is feeding on itself because basically everything that economy is producing, with everything going to the defense sector, is being eaten up and it's not productive. It's one thing if you build a weapon system that gets destroyed a few weeks later. It's another thing if you're investing in roads and bridges and technology. So, I think we're in a place where if we can help in some fashion the Ukrainians to hang on, it'll get to a place where the line is more or less frozen. And the real measure of success is, can Ukraine stand on its own feet militarily, economically, democratically? They're demonstrating that they can do that. Let me ask you, Senator, to one of the Secretary's points he just made. I mean, Ukraine, it's become sort of a laboratory for intelligence and drones and kind of a different way of warfare. Once we get to a point past the "Secretary of maximum lethality," as I'll just call him, and we're in kind of a future point, what lessons do you think can be taken away from this war, and what do you hope that Congress is applying? Well, I'll take it in two directions. First and foremost, yes, absolutely, this is showing this new era of warfare that we're in right now. You know, I'm somebody that grew up in the era where we're concerned about weapons of mass destruction. Now we are finding ourselves in a place where we are trying to navigate these weapons of mass production—this idea of what it is when you're able to produce such quantity, with drones, ballistic missiles. When we have the proliferation of AI, what we see with Mithos and other things like that that are going to completely transform, that is the future. And we do have to be thinking about it both in terms of the warfighter on our side, but also, how do you defend against this? And this is why I was also going over some of our efforts to make sure that we as a nation are ensuring that we are able to do our best to ensure that our adversaries and our competitors like China and Russia are not able to gain some of these tools, whether through AI chips or the means of production to be able to produce those chips. You know, those are the types of things that are going to really determine the stratification of warfare in that way. The last thing I'll just say on this front is I've been very much pushing that we can't just be saying that we're opposed to this "might makes right" approach of foreign policy of Trump, but trying to devise what is our way forward. And I for one believe that that revolves around a singular word, which is resilience. I think we need to focus on an era of resilience in this age of crisis, and to be able to mobilize ourselves to shore up our own vulnerabilities, whether that's critical minerals or pharmaceuticals or energy production or any number of other issues, while we are also trying to make sure that we are performing and fighting on the battlefields of our choosing, and be able to advance on our strengths. So, that's what I think we need to be pushing forward on when we see this incredibly rapidly changing ecosystem that we're in. You know, there's no going back. We need that forward-looking vision and to be able to give a sense of what American credibility and reliability going forward will be based off of. Okay, we only have 5 minutes left, but we've covered a scope of issues I hope all of you cared a lot about. Let me go to you, Ambassador. I would say broadly, if I were to give unofficial broad advice to anybody running for office or president, I would say sit down with really smart people and decide what you think about the really tough issues. So, let me pose this question to you: What do you hope that people who are aspiring to be sitting in the Oval Office are sitting down and spending time mapping out plans and ideas and thoughts with very smart people right now? I hope that they are doing that. And I actually know, I expect that they're sitting down with smart people. But what they really need to do is sit down with the people. They need to get into communities, go into towns, go into capitals, and talk to the people, because it's ordinary people in places like Louisiana who will be voting for them, and if those people are not hearing from them, nothing that smart people say to them will get them elected. What do you wish they would be talking about? Talking about real issues, talking about pocketbook issues, talking about the price of gasoline, talking about their futures—whether their children will have jobs, whether their children will be educated, whether their school boards will be able to pull books like *Roots* out of the library—and stick with it. You know, they want to hear those issues that touch them every day. They don't want to hear the esoteric issues about war and peace, even though those issues have an impact on them. They expect their leaders to know what to do on those issues, but they want their leaders to know what to do to make their lives better. Secretary Blinken, let me ask you the question in a different way because you have helped past people running for president think about issues and kind of red team with them what they think, how to talk about it. What would be top-of-list for you if you were doing that with some candidates right now? You know, there's so much to think about, to focus on. I guess I'd say a few things. One is, and it echoes what Andy's saying and I think what Linda's saying, there's a lot of understandable nostalgia for the past because we had an American century. We had 80 years where, as a result of the work that previous generations did, we were at the front of the parade and shaping things, and those 80 years, with all of their imperfections, profound as they were, we passed that. The reality is you can't put the genie back in the bottle. You've got to shape a new bottle, and that's what these conversations have to all be about. We have a pace and scope of change that is moving at speeds unlike anything I ever experienced before in my 30 years in government. And just by way of one example, to get to 50 million users of the telephone back in the day, it took about 75 years. For radio, it took about 45 years. For TV, it took about 20 years. For Instagram, 4 years. For ChatGPT, 4 months. The challenge that anyone in governance has in dealing with that is acute. We have inequalities among countries that have actually decreased, but within them they've increased, and that has all sorts of repercussions. But finally, and this is what I want to get to, we have a new world order that is being shaped, and grappling with that is going to be the biggest test internationally for future presidents. We have all sorts of emerging actors who are empowered, super-empowered, by technology and by information, and as a result can either advance or disrupt things in ways that they weren't able to before. And that means if you don't have them on the takeoff, you're probably going to have a crash landing. So, as I'm looking forward and I'm thinking about how you advise a president on how to engage the world, of course, it starts with our democratic base and you want strong democracies. I believe that one principle is enduring, and that is enlightened self-interest. The strength and success of others actually redounds to us, and that continues to be important. American engagement and leadership continues to be important. But as we're looking at the way the world is going to be designed, I think increasingly we're going to see alignments of not just countries, but companies, and other actors that have a stake in a given issue coming together around that issue. It's what I used to call variable geometry: different coalitions of different shapes and sizes who are all brought together by a shared interest in a particular issue. Maybe it's dealing with opioids, maybe it's dealing with food security, maybe it's dealing with climate, maybe it's dealing with AI governance. And it won't be a neat autocracy-democracy breakdown, or East-West, or North-South. It'll be like-minded actors on a given issue willing to play by the same set of rules. I think a president has to look at that and think about how he or she organizes the country to engage in that. But it starts with, as we've all been discussing, regaining our credibility, and that's going to be the first task. It's a perfect place to end. I'm very grateful that they are all thinking about these big, difficult issues that I know are on the minds of lots of people here and people watching. Thank you all so much for this panel. Really enjoyed it.

What Trump and Xi Didn't Settle in Beijing, With Nicholas Burns | The President’s Inbox

Council on Foreign Relations(333.0K)·35:26·5.2K views·2026-05-20
Nicholas BurnsU.S. Ambassador to China Donald TrumpPresident of the United States Xi JinpingPresident of China
TL;DRWhile the recent Trump-Xi summit in Beijing established a temporary, detente-like cooling of tensions and yielded modest agricultural and aviation deals, it highlighted deep strategic divides over Iran, technology competition, and a highly controversial potential shift in US Taiwan policy.
TL;DR While the recent Trump-Xi summit in Beijing established a temporary, detente-like cooling of tensions and yielded modest agricultural and aviation deals, it highlighted deep strategic divides over Iran, technology competition, and a highly controversial potential shift in...

TL;DR

While the recent Trump-Xi summit in Beijing established a temporary, detente-like cooling of tensions and yielded modest agricultural and aviation deals, it highlighted deep strategic divides over Iran, technology competition, and a highly controversial potential shift in US Taiwan policy.

Summit Atmosphere, Communication, and Key Economic Outcomes

  • A "Detente-Like" Climate: Former US Ambassador to China Nicholas Burns characterized the summit as highly consequential, noting a visible, visceral cooling of bilateral tensions that have persisted for the last seven to eight years.
  • The Absence of a Joint Communique: Consistent with meetings during the Biden administration, no joint written communique was produced.
      • Profound Differences: The core policy disagreements between Washington and Beijing remain too severe to negotiate a mutually agreed-upon text. Instead, both sides issued separate, mismatched statements highlighting their respective priorities.
  • Key Commercial Agreements:
      • Agricultural Purchases: China committed to purchasing at least $17 billion of US agricultural products for the remainder of the calendar year, and $25 billion annually for the following three years.
      • Aviation Sales: China agreed to purchase 200 Boeing aircraft. While smaller than some analysts anticipated, Burns viewed this as a positive step forward for a vital commercial sector.
      • Tariff and Supply Chain Truce: No official tariff truce was announced. This was likely delayed as the US recalibrates its tariff structures in response to recent Supreme Court decisions challenging executive authority.

Geopolitical Friction: Iran and the Middle East

  • Shared High-Level Rhetoric: China publicly reiterated its long-standing positions that Iran should not become a nuclear power and that the Strait of Hormuz must remain open to free navigation.
  • Limits of Chinese Leverage: Burns expressed skepticism that China would actively pressure Tehran into a ceasefire, particularly if the US resumes threats of military force against Iran.
  • The Authoritarian Axis: China’s closest partners remain Russia, North Korea, and Iran—a coalition actively working together to support Russia's war in Ukraine.
  • Strategic Inaction: China has deliberately avoided taking a proactive role in brokering Middle East peace.
      • Distracting the US: Beijing is content to watch the US remain bogged down in Middle East conflicts, which has forced Washington to divert carrier battle groups and air defense assets away from the Indo-Pacific theater.

The Taiwan Question and Potential US Policy Shifts

  • Xi's Early Warnings: The Chinese delegation took the unusual step of leaking President Xi Jinping's opening private statement mid-morning, firing a warning shot that crossing China's "red lines" on Taiwan could lead to direct conflict.
  • Trump's Air Force One Comments: On his return flight, President Trump equivocated on whether the US would maintain its 50-year-old "One China" policy, hinting that defense arms sales to Taiwan could be used as a bargaining chip.
  • Severe Repercussions of a Policy U-Turn: Burns warned that weakening support for Taiwan would be a historic strategic blunder.
      • Legal Obligations: Defense arms sales to Taiwan are legally mandated by the US Taiwan Relations Act of 1979.
      • Economic Vitality: Taiwan’s advanced semiconductor industry (TSMC) is critical to the global technology supply chain.
      • Damage to Alliances: Abandoning Taiwan would severely erode US credibility with key Indo-Pacific allies (Japan, the Philippines, South Korea, and Australia) and NATO partners, who rely on the reliability of US defense commitments.

Technology Competition and Bilateral AI Regulation

  • The Core Global Battleground: Technology has become the central front in the US-China relationship. Leadership in artificial intelligence, biotechnology, and quantum computing will determine which nation is the dominant global power by the 2030s and 2040s.
  • The "Mythos" AI Wake-Up Call: Anthropic’s advanced "Mythos" model has raised alarm bells. The potential for cyber-terrorists, bio-terrorists, or criminal syndicates to weaponize advanced AI has forced both the US and China to seek bilateral guardrails.
  • Agreement to Negotiate: Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent and President Trump confirmed that the US and China have agreed to launch formal security talks regarding AI risk mitigation.
  • Building on Past Precedent: This follows an agreement brokered at the end of the Biden administration in Lima, Peru, where both countries publicly declared that human beings—not AI systems—must remain in control of nuclear weapons.

Notable Quotes

"One thing I learned in China, Jim, flattery will get you nowhere. It's all about the bottom line." — Nicholas Burns
"If President Trump does make this U-turn, refuse to provide the arms that the Taiwanese have asked for... I think this could be the most significant mistake along with the Iran war of President Trump's second term of office." — Nicholas Burns
"You know the old adage, 'Why interrupt, don't interrupt your enemy while your enemy's making a big mistake.' Napoleon's advice... and the Chinese subscribe to it, so it hasn't surprised me that they haven't tried to be a dealmaker [in the Middle East]." — Nicholas Burns
When the American president sits down with a Chinese leader, it's all business. One thing I learned in China, Jim: flattery will get you nowhere. It's all about the bottom line. Last week, President Donald Trump wrapped up a two-day summit with Chinese leader Xi Jinping. It was the first visit by a US president to China since Trump traveled to Beijing in November 2017. What did Trump and Xi discuss? Did they reach any significant agreements? Did their discussions reset the direction of US-Chinese relations, or merely perpetuate the existing status quo? From the Council on Foreign Relations, welcome to The President's Inbox. I'm Jim Lindsay. Today, I am joined by Nicholas Burns, the Roy and Barbara Goodman Family Professor of the Practice of Diplomacy and International Relations at Harvard University's Kennedy School of Government, and a former US ambassador to the People's Republic of China. Nick, thank you very much for joining me. Jim, thank you so much for letting me be on your show. Let's just jump right into it, Nick. I'd like to sort of get a 40,000-foot view of the summit. I mentioned that you were US Ambassador to China during the Biden administration, and you're also a career Foreign Service officer. You served in senior positions under both Democratic and Republican presidents, so you have staffed a lot of summit meetings over the years, and you've attended a lot of summit meetings over the years. Give me your reaction to what you saw transpire last week. Jim, I think that President Trump's visit to China and his discussions with Chairman Xi Jinping could turn out to be one of the more consequential of the recent summits with China over the last 10 years or so. I say that because there was almost a detente-like atmosphere, a visible, visceral cooling of the tensions that have been so prominent in this relationship for the last seven or eight years. This was true in the last few years of President Trump's first term and certainly throughout the time when I was ambassador for President Biden. And so, it looks like both countries want to dial down the temperature, find a way to have more—more effective channels between them, and find more stability both in the economic relationship and on the many issues that separate us. I think there were a number of accomplishments; I don't agree with those people who say there were no accomplishments. I think there are also a number of areas that worry me greatly, and I'm happy to go into both of those if you'd like. Fair enough, Nick. But before we dive into the particulars, just a couple of big-picture things, just trying to put this summit in context with other summits. My understanding was there was not a final, agreed-upon, written communique at the end of the summit. Is that significant? Is that something that happens at big summits, or not necessarily so? It was expected that there would not be an agreed-upon communique. In fact, throughout the Biden administration, President Biden had three in-person summits, and we never had a joint communique. Why? Because they're too difficult to work out between these two particular governments. The differences are so profound, and it was easier for both sides to put out individual statements, not matching at all. Sometimes they described issues in a different way, sometimes leaving one issue in on one side, and the other side highlighting a different issue. So, it didn't surprise me at all. I don't think people should read too much into that. So help me understand the release of dueling statements, because I read a number of the Chinese statements, at least as translated from Mandarin, and I saw the White House statements. As you hinted at, Nick, they often did not align, and even when they mentioned topics, they tended to emphasize different things. So help me understand, if you were to go back and read those statements, what you should make of them. Well, I thought the American statement was much more specific, in many ways. And, of course, the Chinese statement emphasized the issues that China cares most about, including, most notably, that big shot that Xi Jinping fired across the US bow on Taiwan in the very first hours of the summit. I think, on the positive side, it's very important these two leaders met, and both sides did reflect that in their statements. It was a warm meeting. Xi Jinping sits atop the Chinese firmament as the all-powerful leader, and so you have to meet with him if you want to have any kind of progress with China at all, or even deliver tough messages at the highest level. And so, there's no substitute for head of state communication and symmetry. I thought that was actually an accomplishment of this meeting—that they spent nine hours together, and they were positive about the relationship. On the American side, of course, the US played up the fact that there'll now be a resumption of agricultural sales. China's the largest market for US agriculture. One-fifth of all of our ag exports go to China on an annual basis, and purchases by China have been way down because of the major problems in the relationship. I thought that was positive that, according to James Ing-Grier, the US Trade Rep, the Chinese now have committed to purchase at least $17 billion of US agriculture products for the rest of this calendar year, and 25 billion a year, he said, for the next three years. The Boeing announcement was not as big as people had expected, but since there hadn't been a Boeing sale—and this is a big part of this relationship commercially—200 planes sold by Boeing to China is a good first step. And then third, I thought it was very important that they did not actually announce what everyone thought they might announce: a tariff truce and a supply chain truce. And again, James Ing-Grier indicated, well, because the US tariff picture is changing because of the Supreme Court decision and the other challenges to President Trump's authority, it may be necessary to rearrange some of the tariffs, to recalibrate them this summer. That's a big issue. Neither side wants to go back to the trade and supply chain wars of 2025, and I would hope that they could get that done sometime in the next couple of months. And then finally, what was little noted, but possibly could turn out to be the most important part of this summit, Jim, was what President Trump said on Air Force One when he was returning to the United States, and what Scott Bessent, the Treasury Secretary, said on the first day. The two sides have agreed to sit down and talk about artificial intelligence, and specifically, the security issues that right now are so worrisome. This is especially true after Anthropic's latest model, the Mythos model, indicates that cyber-terrorists, bio-terrorists, and criminal organizations could really use this powerful model for terrible purposes, to upset the international economic system, for instance. So, it makes sense that just as we, the Americans, sat down with the Soviets after the Cuban Missile Crisis, now's the time for these two competitor nations on AI to sit down and talk about our common interest. How do we provide guardrails? How do we introduce mitigating measures to prevent the worst from happening? Okay, Nick. Let's jump into the details, then, of the summit. I want to begin with a topic you haven't mentioned, but seemed to be at the top of President Trump's agenda when he arrived in Beijing, and that is Iran. The President was hoping to get Chinese help in reopening the Strait of Hormuz, presumably also to get Tehran to agree to a deal, although the week before, the Iranian Foreign Minister had visited Beijing. Everybody understands that Beijing has at least some say with Tehran, or at least Tehran is willing to listen. What do you think actually happened on that front, in terms of discussions between Xi and Trump? Yeah, Jim, I thought that the two really worrisome, problematic issues were Iran and Taiwan. Let's take Iran, as you suggest. It was not a surprise to hear that the Chinese were willing to say publicly in Beijing last week, "We are not in favor of Iran becoming a nuclear weapons power." That's been the Chinese position for 20 years. In fact, I was the Iran nuclear negotiator for George W. Bush 20 years ago when China was very much in support of what we were trying to do to deny Iran a nuke. Second, it was also not a surprise to hear the Chinese say—but it was good to hear them say—that the Strait of Hormuz should be reopened without Iran or anybody else as a tollkeeper. But that's been their position, really, since the start of the war. And so, I think it was positive for President Trump that Xi Jinping was willing to say this, and willing to have his team affirm it publicly. I'm somewhat skeptical, however, whether or not China is now going to use its considerable influence over the government in Iran to really push them very, very hard to agree to a ceasefire that the Iranians may not believe is in their interest. And I think this could actually get more complicated if President Trump, in the last couple of days since coming home, has been threatening the Iranians with a resumption of the use of force, with bombing. If that happened, there's really no question in my mind that the Chinese would not react positively; they'd have to criticize it publicly. And I think they'd be unwilling to push the Iranian government to the limit in that instance. And I think the larger point here is, as you know, the Chinese live in an authoritarian world. Their closest partners are Russia—President Putin's visiting Beijing this week, right on the heels of President Trump's visit—North Korea, and Iran. They're all working together to support the Russian war effort in Ukraine, for instance. And so, I do think there's a limit to what the Chinese are going to do to help us. Nick, are you surprised at all that Xi Jinping hasn't tried to insert China into the Middle East situation? Remember, a few years ago, a lot was made of the fact that Beijing helped broker rapprochement between Tehran and Riyadh. Obviously, a lot of Americans worry about China eclipsing the United States in world politics, becoming the dominant superpower going around setting the rules of the game. At least in terms of the current US war or ceasefire with Iran, we're not seeing the Chinese proactively trying to erect a peace. Is there a reason for that? Well, it's interesting you bring up, Jim, the fact that in 2023, three years ago, Foreign Minister Wang Yi presided over a ceremonial handshake, a coming together of the Iranian and Saudi foreign ministers in Beijing. I remember talking to the Chinese Foreign Ministry in the aftermath, and they were pretty smug and feeling good about the fact: "You know, we are now a new Middle East power." But to answer your question, I think one of the reasons why China has not asserted itself is because of the image that China really could do nothing to protect the Iranians on February 28th, even politically, and afterwards. They also did not protect Nicolas Maduro on January 3rd when the United States kidnapped him and brought him to the United States. I think this has been a hit on China's reputation, if you will, for reliability in the Global South. So, that's reason number one. Reason number two, I think the Chinese have also felt, if the United States can get caught in the quicksand of the Middle East again, why would we help them get out of the quicksand? Because the Chinese have seen that we've had to transfer a carrier battle group from Japan to the Middle East, and that very important air defense systems have been transferred from our bases in Korea, the Philippines, and Japan to the Middle East theater. You know the old adage: "Don't interrupt your enemy while your enemy's making a big mistake." Napoleon's advice. Napoleon's. And the Chinese subscribe to it, so it hasn't surprised me that they haven't tried to be a dealmaker. I think they probably recognize they've got enough influence behind the scenes with Pakistan, because, of course, they are very close to Pakistan. And so, I think the Chinese have been comfortable not to be in the forefront of this. So, we've talked about one T, Nick, that is Tehran. Let's talk about the other T: Taiwan. He was quite clear in terms of how China viewed the issue. But obviously, there's been a lot of reporting about the comments that President Trump made on his flight back from Beijing about what US policy will be going forward toward Taiwan. So, how do you sort of see that issue? What was interesting, Jim, to me, was that the Chinese actually leaked President Xi's opening statement in the private meeting on the first day, which was that shot that he fired across—the proverbial shot that he fired across our bow—that if you cross our red lines—this is to Donald Trump and the United States—if you cross our red lines, then there's a possibility of a conflict. That's a very strong statement. Xi Jinping always raises Taiwan. This is one of the stronger statements I've heard him make in the last four or five years. And they leaked it mid-morning before they'd even left the meeting. They wanted that out there; they wanted that to dominate the meeting. It's been reassuring to hear Secretary of State Rubio and Ambassador David Perdue—and I have a lot of respect for Ambassador Perdue, by the way, he's doing a really good job, I think, as ambassador in China—they've both said, "Look, there's no change to our Taiwan policy." But how do you square that with what President Trump said on Air Force One on the way home? He, in that one conversation with journalists, equivocated on whether or not the United States would stick to our One China policy and its Taiwan dimensions of the last 50 years. He questioned the whole policy of recognizing One China, strategic ambiguity about what we would do in the event of a Chinese attack, and particularly, this issue of our legal requirement to provide defensive military technology to Taiwan—that's in the Taiwan Relations Act. All that happened in the space of three or four minutes, and I was watching it live, and I thought, this is a possible very dramatic, and I think, very ill-advised U-turn. This would give the Chinese what they've been trying to get for a half-century since President Nixon sat down with Mao and Zhou Enlai in February of 1972. I was just thinking, as a former ambassador to NATO, and also, while I was ambassador to China, someone who spent a lot of time working with Japan, our allies, Korea, the Philippines, and Australia, what's the signal here to the allies? Is the commitment and word of the United States good anymore? Because if we do not proceed with the arms sales, if that's going to be President Trump's position—and we'll have to see what he decides to do on it, he hasn't made a final decision, but it sure looked like he was leaning in that direction—I think that the allies are going to be significantly worried about the staying power and reliability of the United States. This is not to speak of the fact that there are 23 or 24 million Taiwanese who live in a really strong democracy, who want to be left alone, and who don't want to be, you know, the latest acquisition by the People's Republic of China. How are they going to feel about this? I think this could turn out, if President Trump does make this U-turn and refuses to provide the arms that the Taiwanese have asked for and that we suggested they buy from us to strengthen their defense, to be the most significant mistake, along with the Iran war, of President Trump's second term of office. There's a lot there, Nick, and I want to sort of just dig a little deeper. I want to begin first with your comments about the significance of what Xi said about Taiwan, and that they led with it and leaked it. What I'm trying to sort of parse through is the extent to which Xi had this leaked—and I assume nothing gets leaked without his permission—to, in essence, throw a fastball high and inside to the Americans, versus throwing red meat to domestic hardliners in China so he could sort of inoculate himself against concerns that he isn't being tough enough on Taiwan. Do you have any sort of sense of why it was leaked? The Chinese always raise Taiwan—every Chinese interlocutor does. Even when I used to go see the Agriculture Minister or the Health Minister, I would get the Taiwan lecture sometime in the first five minutes of the meeting. So, I always expected it; I always had my response. But President Xi, in all the meetings that I've been in with him over the last four years, always raised Taiwan in a very tough and nearly uncompromising manner, so I wasn't surprised to see this. The Chinese in the past have leaked certain things in the middle of meetings, which, by the way, kind of violates diplomatic protocol. You don't really—you're not supposed to one-up the people you're meeting with before they've had a chance to issue their own statement or speak to the press after the meeting. But they did it because I believe they think that President Trump is susceptible to this kind of intimidation. That was a statement by Xi Jinping meant to intimidate the President of the United States and our country, and we should not be intimidated by the Chinese on Taiwan. And that means going forward with the arms sales. That means reassuring the Japanese and Filipinos, the two allies closest to Taiwan, that we're going to stand with them here.
